RATING: 9
Near perfect game. My only complaint is that downtime can be long since you are waiting for everyone else to go.

It is much like Kingsburg in space with a lot more options. You aren't really penalized by low rolls since each area of the board can use something different. 

I love that the scoring is so small. It makes it really easy to keep track of.

I can see putting a lot of plays on this!

RATING: 6

RATING: 7
Worker placement and area control mesh together nicely in this game that reminds me a little bit of Kingsburg.  The Space theme is presented very well, although the players are left having to create their own story behind the game.  The game designates the dice as your actual spaceships, docking at orbital stations around the moon of an alien planet in a struggle for domination.  I've found it to be somewhat intimidating for new players.  The rules are simple, making it easy to teach, but one player can easily "run away" with the game if the others do not take the appropriate strategic measures to stop them.  This might make it too cat & mousy for some.

RATING: 6
1 play / 4 players

LIKE: Many interesting combos, area control that gives powers. Great use of dice.
DISLIKE: Extremely high downtime between turns. Also, there is are kingmaking and trounce the leader issues.
OVERALL: Late game gets vicious as players volley for area control and start stealing from each other. The game is fun, but it needs some adjustments. I would place (in addition to the 6th colony) a set number of turns before the game ends. Otherwise the game could go on and on. Also, I would ban "takebacks" to speed up turns.

RATING: 6
+Nice use of dice as workers.
+Numerous ways to mitigate luck of the dice.
+Seems well balanced (no dominant strategy).
+Enjoyable artistic style.

-Down time can be a problem in 4 player games. DO NOT PLAY WITH 5 (requires expansion, but don't do it)! Players cannot do any planning when it is not their turn, so you are left sitting there doing nothing, and turns take longer because there is no pre-planning.  I will never play this with more than 3 players again.
-Too much "pile on the leader" for my taste.
-Games seem to always end with very close scores (see previous point).  Because scores are low (around 7-9), they are always close, which makes it very easy for the game to end up in a king-making scenario.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
FAVOURITE GAME - this is one of my all-time favourites. It is a worker placement/dice rolling game, where you have to conquer a central moon by building colonies on it. To do this, you have to allocate your ships to perform action of resource gathering, building, trading and so on. The ships are your dice. You roll a number of dice/ships and accordingly you distribute them between the actions. It is a light and not so long space conquest game accesibble for everyone. The trick is that you do not recover your dice at the end of your turn so you are able to block someone else's strategy plans, and as it, the game has a lot of interaction.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Aesthetically, I like my space games in extremes. I don't mind your games like [thing=39339][/thing] that take themselves seriously, but I'm just as drawn to [thing=142079][/thing] that doesn't take anything seriously. What I can't stand is a game in-between. Alien Frontiers is a bit like this, with it's naming of planets after famous writers and disjointed thematic schema. Looking at, and playing the game, I was repelled by the hokey layout and boxy art. 

Mechanically, there are some interesting things going on, but nothing groundbreaking. Dice Allocation seems great - rolling 1s can be good! - but what tends to happen in a lot of dice allocation games is that in an attempt to balance the numbers, choices become dictated or meaningless. Here, this isn't always the case, but most of the time you can do what you want and are trying to gouge value from the leftovers. 

Dynamically, this causes huge problems. Downtime just destroys this game. You can't plan properly until you've rolled, and you can't roll until your turn. This is intractable, as you need to know where others played to, so you can't play simultaneously. Further, the endgame has no drama as players are pulled back from the brink of victory simply because it's the right play of whomever is still in the game, so winning is mostly being the last man standing ... after several hours of dithering. 

I don't think this game is pointless, but I also don't think it is better than games that most people already have. As one of the first Kickstarter successes, this should have been a standard bearer for the problems of crowdfunded games (poor art, weak development, redundancy).

I'D RATHER WE PLAYED: [thing=13][/thing]. These games are very similar in tempo, but with City-building there is an inevitable surge through the endgame that overcomes analysis paralysis and kingmaking scenarios.

RATING: 8
The game takes a playing or two to get your head around all the stuff you can do and how to stop other players from doing what they want to do. And those first couple games might last a little longer than you might like. But after that the game should move along nicely. There are lots of options and different routes to victory which is nice. For every way there is for someone to gain victory points or screw you over there is generally a counter in the game somewhere which adds some much needed balance. In the first couple games you might experience a bit of a run away leader issue as the players are still exploring these options. All in all a solid game. Probably best with 3 or 4 players but playable with 2. The rules are very easy to explain which makes getting into the game a lot quicker than in similar games.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.8
Most of the good things that you've heard about Alien Frontiers are true! But now for the negatives:
-the setting of the game is in the middle of the dense Analysis Paralysis Nebula.....
-while it's quite a good game, Alien Frontiers is a little long for what it offers. This is due to AP and how the game ends: the game immediately ends when your last colony is placed. Obviously, if you're not winning, you're sure not gonna place your last colony! And you'll definitely know if you're winning or not because there is no end-of-game final scoring: it's absolutely clear who is in the lead.
Neither a positive or a negative, I must disagree with those who say that the rules are a breeze. They're not; it will all make sense after one play, but it's kinda heavy when you're just reading through them.

RATING: 5
PROS
- Intuitive board, once it's explained to you. The dice placement spots makes sense and works together. 
- Alien tech cards are fun and "breaks" rules of the game in cool ways
- Interaction with other players with option to screw them over too
- The dice are nice and big to roll 
- Area control is great to do and adds another level to the game 

CONS
- Downtime is a bitch! You have nothing to do when it's not your turn besides wait for the other players to go. 
- Analysis Paralysis is very common. If you have a player that takes a long time for turns, it's probably going to take even longer in this game. So many combos you can do to try and get what you want while others wait. 
- Bash the leader near the end. Whoever might win, everybody tries and gangs up on them so it gives off a bad vibe for the game where "teams" are not a part of the game.

RATING: 6
One play, 2 players. The beginning of the game felt curiously broken, as I was blocked out of obtaining additional workers by my opponent two turns in a row, and then his dice advantage let him easily get the straight needed to steal the resources I had been accumulating in the meantime. I was able to climb back in via using a couple of plasma rifles to blow away two of his dice, and then make use of the cheaper colony constructor due to a power up. Holographic decoy helped make sure none of my key cards got stolen either. Overall fairly interesting but I think newer dice placement games have improved upon it pretty significantly, though one play is perhaps not enough to give a fair judgement

RATING: 5.5
After a bit of time with this game, I feel ... conflicted.
I don't hate it, but there is something about it which also stops me from really liking it.  I'm glad to have it because it's a fairly casual game with some cool combo elements and thinking involved, so it can appeal to everyone.
The problem, I think, is the end game.  There is too much control for the winning player to decide when to end the game, and since all values are known a person can decide to 'win now' or not.  In almost every case it has been the player who goes first that has won.

Alien frontiers is usually fun while you're playing it, and then falls flat on the finish.  There's little tension or little of one player gaining any real power (it's almost too easy to be crumbled if the other players want to do so) to pull ahead.

On the flip side, with all the cool tech cards that let you manipulate the dice and board positions, it really is the most fun dice game I've played.  Again, it's just the final turn or two that make it such a damper.

RATING: 7
Another interesting "[thing=2243][/thing] on steroids" style game.  Here players are space colonists fighting for control over various regions of a planet.  Feels kind of like [thing=37380][/thing] in that after you roll your dice you see how to best manage them to claim various goals and benefits.  Features a lot of player interaction which manifests in a number of ways (stealing resources, jockeying for colony position, simply blocking desired actions from others, etc.).  The components are fantastic, with beautiful art (both on the board and cards) and well though out bits.  I do however have a little issue with the non-standard box size in that it doesn't really stack up well next to boxes like [thing=36218][/thing] nor [thing=9209][/thing] nor games like [thing=31260][/thing] (it does however stack up decently with smaller square boxed games like [thing=74][/thing] and [thing=25669][/thing]).  The rules are really easy to understand and once completely understood, the game plays smoothly and quickly.  I only wish the ending were a bit more climatic as towards the end-game it can feel fairly obvious as to who will win (which results in a lot of bash the leader and a little king-making).  Overall a great light to medium weight game which appeals to a wide array of gamers and non-gamers alike.

RATING: 7
Kingsburg with a sci-fi theme? Well, you're pretty close but also a bit far off with that description . There are a few differences that make it a whole different bird. Yes. You still allocate resources to build up your particular kingdom/empire. But the worker (dice) placement here can be much more cutthroat. You can very often get blocked out of what you really wanted and have no real recourse. In this game you have the Alien Artifact location which allows you to get cards with certain abilities that can really swing things in your direction. Except for the Leader cards that come with To Forge a Realm there isn't anything like that in Kingsburg. This one also has the ability to hugely mess with your opponent in a fashion other than just taking the stuff they wanted. The Raider's Outpost is certainly very close to an attack option but there are ways to mitigate it. The biggest difference of course is the area control aspect of the game. This not only determines the scoring, it gives you awesome abilities later on though other players can battle it out with you for them. All in all Alien Frontiers is an awesome game. Had we not already owned Kingsburg this game might rate a 10 but we so love Kingsburg that its hard to place this complex sibling higher.

RATING: 7
The roll of two to six dice determine what actions you can do. Strategically make plans and contingency plans based on your roll. The game can take a long time because you never know what you can do until you roll your dice. No matter how good your plans are, if you don't get the roll that you need, you might end up making moves that pretty much waste your turn. This is a good game for people who like to think on their feet and can see many scenarios and determine a course of action quickly.
Castles of Burgundy plays like Alien Frontiers, but shorter.  I would choose that over Alien Frontiers because it's way less complicated and shorter playing/learning time. Having said that, I still like Alien Frontiers for the more advanced players.
Right now, I'm really into games that use dice to determine actions because they add "luck" factor.

Not a big deal, just a bit disappointing. Anyways, the game played really well. I thought that I had it in the bag and everyone turned their attention to me and the player right behind me won. RATING: 6
This could be the best dice game in the vein of Kingsburg, Airships, etc. that I've played.  Could.  But eh, I gotta go with Airships.  My key hangup is downtime.  I hate waiting around for people to map out the dice usage for their turn, and expect them to hate waiting around for me too.  We played with 3P, I'm pretty sure I'd never want to play the game with more, and I still felt like the downtime was excessive.  This can be player-dependent, of course, but playing by gut can be disastrous in this game because of the clever opportunities that exist.  Because players' dice block the board, they can't pre-roll between turns.  And using proxy dice for the blockers would seem awkward to me.  I want this game to be an 8, but I think it's a 6 for me.

RATING: 8.5
I have been pleasantly surprised by this game at each turn.  Initially, the design was interesting enough to pull me in, the 50's style Sci-Fi art is fantastic and really looks great on the table.  The graphic design (icons, layout, etc) is very clear and top notch.  When I got to actual game play, turn 2 hit and suddenly the light game was showing some real interesting decisions.  It became clear that there are several factors in the game that the players really can exploit.  The key factors are ships, territory benefits, alien tech cards, and the 3 special projectors.  First play focused on the ships quickly with territory and tech a close second.  But subsequent play revealed that all 4 of these must be utilized to ensure the best results.  Its easy to set aside the discard functions of the cards or the use of the projectors as 'too expensive', but that really sells the game short.  The balance of all of these factors really provides a solid depth of play that isn't expected in a "dice game".  the game seems to buck the trend that help one's self is better than neutralizing an opponent - and that really makes the game play out quite interestingly,  The game is very well made; except the dice appear to be a little shy of square.  I haven't measured this, but to the eye they appear off.  All other aspects of the game are very good.  Glad I purchased my copy.  Already looking forward to expansions. [Oct '10]

Update: Upped rating after many more plays.  A really easy game to teach and a game that scales well with player skills.  Dice concerns also eliminated.  Very solid game in both play and components. [Mar '11]

RATING: 5
I really like the 1950's space artwork in this one, and wanted it to do more for me than it did.  

This will inevitably be compared to Kingsburg.  For me the preference is for Kingsburg over this.  You always place your dice after the person to your right, which means that you're always dependent on their play style.  I prefer Kingsburg's mechanism where you rotate based on the number of pips they have on that roll.

What really brings the game down for me is the amount of bash the leader and 'take that' that you see with this game.  Towards the end it's about reining in the leader rather than executing on your own strategy.  Will play again to see if my impressions change.

RATING: 5.8
I wanted to really love this game; it has gorgeous components, and a wonderful theme. Initial gameplay is also interesting, and full of potential. The game however suffers from two pretty fundamental problems: 
1) Complete inability to plan ahead during opponent's turns, since you won't be able to roll your dice until your turn (and to a lesser degree, since the locations where you want to place them might only be blocked or opened up on any other player's turn). Having a second set of "planning" dice (and pre-determined houserules for what to do when you lose one of your dice after rolling these) is essential for speeding up the game to bearable levels.
2) Perfect information about the score of all players upon reaching the end game condition, leading players to play to prevent a win by anyone else, thus causing the game to drag on beyond the natural ending point.

Late-game turns take a long time, since the possession of many dice, and many alien tech cards, will mean many different ways of potentially altering dice, and thus many paths to explore for the optimal way to use the dice. Meanwhile other players are going to be bored out of their skulls waiting for this process.

On the positive side: Despite lots of dice rolling, there's very little luck factor influencing the outcome of the game. Very well done that, inviting comparisons to Troyes.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I had been really looking forward to this, but was a little disappointed.  I think the basic game is excellent, but the cards add too much "chaos" to the game.  They also extend the game significantly, as folk take two or three attempts to work out which order they need to do things to get the best result.  Perhaps if I were more familiar with the cards, it would flow better, but I'm not sure.  And they're probably necessary as a catch-up mechanism.  I'd be more than happy to try this again, but I would use a timer (60 secs?) to move things along somewhat.

RATING: 7

I would just echo the point made elsewhere that it's best not played with someone who takes a long time over their turn since there's only limited planning that you can do between turns.

RATING: 8
First play was a 2-player game with Trisha and a lot of fun.  On paper AF looks like it might be just another Kingsburg or Stone Age but there is so much "cool" stuff in AF to make it more fun.  The cards are cool.  the territories are cool.  the orbital platforms are cool.  there's lots of opportunities to chain some clever moves together.  

The big thing is that it seems to strike a good balance between letting the dice have some effect on the game without making it feel like they were totally taking it over.

We both liked it a lot!

Update:  I've now played AF a few more times and with more players.  I will say that down-time CAN be an issue while the active player shift their dice around from one possibility to the next, but on the whole this has alwayws been a really fun game with lots of direct interaction and competition between all involved.

RATING: 8
Szuper ez a játék!!! Mielőtt kipróbáltam, csak ennyit hallottam mindenfelől: "Jobb, mint a Kingsburg!" Nos  szerintem az Alien Frontiers annyira magasan veri a Kingsburgot, hogy teljesen más szintet képviselnek.  A Kingsburgban amúgy is hiányoltam a dinamikát (ugyanaz zajlik végig, lehetne 3-szor olyan hosszú, vagy rövidebb, semmi sem változna), de ez után a játék után kifejezetten laposnak érzem a Kinsburgot, pedig nem olyan rossz játék úgy önmagában véve az se. Inkább az Al. Frontiers a túl jó! Egyértelműen toronymagasan a legjobb kockás, amivel valaha játszottam. Az áradozás után egy kis konkrétum, hogy miért is tetszik annyira (pedig csak kettest próbáltam, milyen lehet a 3-4 fős??):
Először is egyszerű a rendszere, mégis sok dilemma elé állít minket, ez általában jó egy játéknál. A kockák felhasználási helyei ötletesek, nincs nagyon rossz dobás,a bónuszlapkák és a kártyák is jók, nagyon jól össze van gyúrva az egész. Elég interakció, konfrontáció is van a játékban (kártyalopás, helyek elfoglalása). Nehéz elhúzni, és ha le is maradok, nincs az az érzésem, hogy teljesen esélytelen, és elmegy a kedvem is az egésztől.
Nem mellékesen a játék szép, ikonrendszere is jó! Nyelvfüggőek ugyan a kártyák, és a lapkák, de összesen 20-30 mondat az egész (12 féle kártya, 8 lapka).
Mindenkinek ajánlom, nemcsak a kockajátékok kedvelőinek. Lehet, hogy még javítani is fogok a 9 ponton.
Szegény Kingsburg, ezután nehezen fog előkerülni. Meg a Troyes is, Alea is...
Amit majdnem elfelejtettem: egyszer sem merült fel a játék során, hogy "jaj de mákod volt", "jaj de pechem volt". Tudom, hogy kocka=szerencse, de a sok lehetőség miatt nem annyira szerencsefüggő a játék, mint azt az ember gondolná. Ha az ellenfél túl jó kártyához jut, ellophatjuk tőle, ha rosszat dobunk, viszonylag olcsón módosíthatjuk (sok kártya van, ami erre szolgál), dinamikus a játék, nincs benne szinte semmi, ami végleges!
---
4-es is jó!! Nagyon tuti ez a rendszer, aminek a lényege, hogy a többiek kockája mindig foglalja a helyet (de azért kártyákkal lehet ez ellen tenni).
Saját bőrömön tapasztaltam, hogy a pontazonosság esélye nagyon nagy a játékban, 4 fő esetén szinte biztos, és ilyenkor a kártyák száma dönt. Ezzel nagyon felértékelődnek a kártyák, erre fontos odafigyelni. Hiába áll valaki látszólag jól kolóniák terén, ha valaki szerez váratlanul egy 1 pontot érő kártyát. Ha egy játékban akár 6 összpontszámmal nyerni lehet, ott bizony nüanszok döntenek:)
---
Nem érzem elég kompenzációnak az elején azt az 1-2 nyersanyagot a kezdővel szemben. Ha a végén a kezdő fejezi be, és végig ugyanannyi kockával dobott mint a többiek, akkor mivel eggyel több köre volt, akár 6-tal több kockával dobhatott a játék során, ami jelenthet akár 18 nyersanyagot is. Persze ez a dilemma a legtöbb "azonnal vége" játéknál fennáll.

RATING: 8.2
I love dice, hence the love for this one. Simple and elegant in its retro look..The 2p game is somewhat non-restrictive in terms of acts you might be able to take (most of the time anyway). I trust that with more players it has to be really tight. Will check in due time..
UPDATE: Definitely my favorite medium weight dice game. Love the feel, the looks, the different ways to win. You never feel like you can do ABSOLUTELY NOTHING important with your dice (as is the case with other games of the genre). Yes, the luck factor from rolling dice IS there so if you prefer to have complete control over your actions, stay away from the game. If not, you will enjoy this immensely, even though it can get a little AP-prone since you cannot really plan your turn in advance (you roll your dice at the start of your turn). Overall, if you like dice, worker placement or better their combination, this game is fun in spades in a box.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Rating is with the Factions and expansion/promo packs listed below mixed into the base game.

Alien Frontiers (Fifth Edition) is an easy to learn, cleverly designed game that also presents itself attractively. The game is mostly Ameritrash dressed up in a well-tailored European suit. Its dressings, however, made me initially approach Alien Frontiers with a euro-game sensibility, but I was quickly disabused of that notion. This game is about the dice-rolling, the combo-building, the area control, the resource/technology stealing, and the colony-building race. These mechanisms come together to form a playground that allows a dice-based fight over territories. Alien Frontiers can be a great fun time, and seems best suited for higher player counts. Its probably best for a group of 3-4 friends (or family) that want to banter among themselves more than fret over the strategic nuances of a board game. 

However, downtime between turns can be a problem for those higher player counts, so it really depends on the people involved in the game session. While it makes zero sense to plan your next turn during the downtime, Alien Frontiers makes you interested in what other people are rolling and doing on their turn (e.g., are they going to take the 3, 4 and 5, and steal my best technology card?!), so the downtime isn't abhorrent. Still, there is a potential for players to lack focus on placing colonies onto the board, and this can also add time to the game session. There is potential for Alien Frontiers to overstay its welcome. 

The player factions seem only OK. Some of them are boring and, depending on the specific power, the applicability in a particular game session can be limited to only certain portions of the game. I could see playing this game without the factions and being fine.

The agenda cards of the Factions expansion, however, are a great fit for the game, and I doubt I'd play it without the agendas.

For my collection, this game will compete most closely with Lords of Waterdeep for table time. The Skullport module of Lords of Waterdeep pushes that game up a bit over this one in my estimation. 

Promos/Extras/Expansions:
Alien Frontiers: Factions (2012)
Alien Frontiers: Expansion Pack #1 (2015)
Alien Frontiers: Expansion Pack #2 (2015)
Alien Frontiers: Promo Pack 2017 (2017)

RATING: 7.5
Very good first impression (rating has good chances to be upgraded after some more plays):

+ Short playing time (less than 90 minutes for 4 first time players)
+ Some of the easiest rules I've ever encountered; Gameflow can be explained in three sentences.
+ Nontheless a complex game
+ Good balance between different options 
+ Territory boni and Alien cards are well intergrated (important for winning and not simply thematic chrome)
+ Great player interaction for a Euro
+ Iconography is brilliant (so there will be no rulebrowsing when trying to find out 
+ Great artwork and cool theme
+ Works with 4 as well as with 2 players (haven't tried 3 so far)

- Slight downtime problems
- Early bad luck hinders development enormously (no doubles = no 4th ship = pita)

RATING: 8
With experienced players: 10
With new players: 7

Now, not to rip on new players.  It's just that kicking each other in the shins is such an integral part of really enjoying this game, it's hard to both:
a) introduce people to the game
b) tell them that kicking and being kicked in the shins is the fun of it.

Like, where do you draw the line?  I mean, if all they think they can do is harvest ore or energy, it's a boring, boring game.  Likewise though, you don't want to go all raid-happy on them the second round, either.  So it takes a bit of trust to really learn this game, and then you don't figure out what the fun things you can do are, until late in the second half of the game.

6-23-11 Traded:

The short story:  I got sick of always introducing it...to myself.  So whenever I played it, I'd never be good enough to have a sense of knowing what I was doing.

It's a good game, but there's a sweet spot when everyone has had 5-10 plays of it, that this game is not going to hit---considering that I either play with gamers or non-gamers.  Either they don't play any games, or they essentially play too many other games to get good at this one game.  When it has the ubiquity of Stone Age, it will be awesome...  it will also be easy to find at a game day then, too.

The first game is, a first game.  This will suffer from not fully understanding the options laid-out before you worse than normal, but do remember that when you know what you're doing, there's quite a bit of conflict.

Games 2-5, I like to think of as "Hungry Hungry Hippos" with dice.  You're just sort of grabbing stuff as you can grab it, with only a vague sense of direction.

Turns 1-5 : getting to 5 ships.  Anything remaining goes to getting alien tech cards or the bare minimum resources you need to get those 5 ships.
Turns 6-? : With alien tech cards, you can do darn near anything you want on any turn you want.  This is where the Hungry Hungry Hippos shows-up.  Need resources, you can get resources.  Need colonies?  You can get colonies.  ...and eventually so many rule-breaks and special abilities show up that you can't keep track of all your own rules anyway.  "Oh crap, I forgot about this card".  "Oh crap, I forgot about this ability".

So I just end up grabbing crap, one base is just as good as any other.  If there's any design flaw, it's that they give you a TON of stuff, all potentially useful, to do with your dice, regardless of the roll.

Now, it's still good.  While you can go anywhere almost regardless of the dice roll, it doesn't mean you SHOULD go anywhere.   ...and that's where you really need to have most of the folks at the table (myself included) really know the game, all the rule break cards, all the colonies.  Strategic use of the raider's outpost (it's not just for revenge anymore), knowing what properties are hot and what are hotter, and the list goes on.

Without really knowing the game, I didn't feel any sense of direction nor was I interested in what everyone else was doing.  No, 'oh crap, they got that colony\alien tech card' or 'yes, I got the whatever!' (relic ship is still cool).  I was just sort of going through the motions, once the dice mechanics of the game lost their newness.  If I can trust that this game will see play 10-15 times with mostly the same people, it will definitely find it's way back into the collection.

RATING: 8.6
A Dice based, worker placement, resource collection, area control game! In space! 

Lots of clever mechanisms in place here, but the game is pretty simple at heart. The key bit comes in working out how to chain your turn together to best utilise the dice pips you have available to you. As in if I place the "4" dice here, I get two wafer resources, I spend those to use this card to flip that "5" dice up one and that "3" dice down one, so now I have 2 "6" dice which I can place to get 2 ore resources and now I have enough ore to place those two "3" dice to get another ship! 

Plenty of attacking other players, the natural urge to stop the leader and the chance someone can come from behind with a late run if you're not watching carefully. Doesn't last to long either. 

Great fun!

RATING: 7

However, I found the end-game disappointing. I need to play again, but I got the feeling that whoever places their last colony will almost always win because there are so few VPs available, usually in the end game a point scored also removes one from an opponent, and by then you have enough tech cards to manipulate the colony position in a way that you win. This changes the game into a simple race to get colonies built, which takes some of the enjoyment out of it for me. I also think there is a risk of too much downtime between turns, and if I'm honest the game lasts just a little bit too long for what you get out of it. Good but not brilliant.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
This is become one of my favourite games.   Goal of the game is to build colonies on the centre planet space.   A bit of a race will you, but being the player that places their last colony only ends the game, not wins the game.   

Once the game ends, the player with the most points wins.   There are a few, but limited, ways to get point with most games ending with a winning score of 12 to 16 points.

The main mechanism in the game is to roll dice (your ships), using the resulting faces of the dice (1 - 6) and place them accordingly to gather resources, move a colony launcher, build more dice, buy technology cards, of...steal stuff from your opponent(s), my favourite action.  

It is a pretty straight forward game, with a little bite, and tight game mechanisms.

I did like the theme.  I really enjoyed the naming of the colonies after famous sci-fi authors.  And the production art was very nice and retro.  The game is long on theme but short on fun.

RATING: 7
I thought it would be a rethemed Kingsburg, but after I read the rules I found out that I was totally wrong and the only thing they have in common is that you are rolling dice and using them as workers.

The theme is absolutely awesome. I really like that fifties-sixties Sci-Fi look. It's great. And to name the areas on Mars after great Sci-Fi-authors is a superb idea. Love it.

The game feels better than Kingsburg since you don't have that runaway-leader problem, because the points that matter are calculated at the end. The only flaw with that is that there is no hidden scoring. If you look at the board you get an idea of who is standing where. Maybe there is an expansion for that.

I personally like it more than Kingsburg, by now. But maybe that will change with an expansion on either game.

RATING: 8
Impressed after one play.  Beautiful graphic design, relatively simple rules, clear iconography, lots of choices -- so far, looks promising.

Repeated play is so far very satisfying.  Quick and simple to teach, 90% of the rules are indicated on the board, and the few grey areas we've encountered are clearly spelled out in the rules, with examples.  Remarkably well-balanced.  Basically, a streamlined Kingsburg in space, with little to no AP.

My only concern is that at some point it will start to feel scripted; at some times, there's clearly a best play, but at others, the decisions are nervewracking.  (I'm doing myself a favor and avoiding strategy articles on this one.)

RATING: 7
I really like Alien Frontiers.  The mechanics are delightful and the artwork/production are beautiful.  The game is easy to explain but offers lots of depth and fun choices.

That said, I'm not loving it quite as much as I first did.  The "worker-placement with dice" mechanic and the fact that your dice must stay on the board until your next turn mean that you have no way to plan ahead.  You must roll your dice and make all your decisions while other players are waiting.

Experienced players can map out a few possibilities ahead of time, but new players will always take quite a while to make their moves.  With AP-prone folks, turns can be rather torturous, especially if they have a few tech cards.

Still, with the right group, this game is exceptional.

RATING: 3
After an additional play, my rating is definitely dropping on this one.  The first exploratory game was fun, but I felt like the options just plain seemed limited and obvious the second go-round.

The good... I really enjoy worker placement games, and the die rolling plus worker placement is right up my alley!  I also like that, while rolling what you need is really going to help you, the alien tech cards allow you greater control over your rolls, such as allowing you add or subtract a pip, or flip a die over.  This reduces the luck factor a bit and is a very nice touch!

The bad... the game is way too rubber-bandy.  If someone jumps out to a lead, the other players have a lot they can do to harm that player until the scores even up again.  I very much dislike this element of the game, since an early lead just means you get ganged up on until the scores are evened, and the scores end up so tight that it's a total shot in the dark who will eventually end up winning.

The tacked-on-feeling area majority portion of the game greatly contributes to this.  It is way too simple to tie up the leader in his areas to rope him back in.  Once each region has a colony in it, its just a matter of who is in the lead that needs to have a point stolen from them.  Yawn.  Essentially, area majority in games works great when a lot of pieces are being played and/or the pieces in play are fairly fluid.  However, in Alien Frontiers, the pieces in play are both few and almost totally static, making it a poor mechanic to include in this particular game.

Another kick in the pants to this game is that the beginning of the game is terrible.  The obvious goal early in the game is to get more ships.  However, this depends on whether you can roll a pair on three dice.  Sure, if you're unlucky, you can go for an alien tech card that removes the luck factor a bit, allowing you to get the pair on your next turn, but those fortunate enough to add to their fleet early have a huge leg up, simply because they were able to roll a pair on their first turn.  In addition, the starting player has absolutely no chance of getting a ship on the first turn due to the start player handicapping, which is a rather large disadvantage.

I like to have various strategic options in this game, and in the early game, there is simply no option other than to build your fleet (if you'd like to have a shot at winning).  Since more ships = more options and actions, there are simply no other viable starting options other than building a fleet, which there is only one way to do.  I would wager that it's impossible to win if you don't do this early on.  So, until a fair bit into the game, everyone is trying to do the exact same thing, which doesn't sit right with me.

In the end, if you like rolling dice and you like worker placement, give it a try.  I don't think the entire game is terrible by any means, but I found it has too many elements I dislike, plus some elements that I perceive as flaws in the game design to make it worthwhile to me.  Obviously, there are a lot of people that enjoy it, so if the things that bother me aren't things that would bother you, go for it!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 4
Updated 10/30/10.

[thing=48726]Alien Frontiers[/thing] probably doesn't deserve this spot on this list just yet.  But I have a soft spot for small-publisher games.  I also suspect that two-player Alien Frontiers is not the game's strong suit.  Strike that.  I suspect that two-player Alien Frontiers may simply be unplayable.  

My disappointment with Alien Frontiers is that it is essentially a tableau game -- like [thing=28143]Race for the Galaxy[/thing] -- masquerading as a worker-placement and area-control game.  I’m not generally a fan of tableau games, and in this case, the tableau causes several problems that affirmatively detracts from the game. 

For the unfamiliar, Alien Frontiers uses dice in a manner not unlike [thing=27162]Kingsburg[/thing], essentially a roll-and-place action selection system.  Unlike other dice games such as Kingsburg and [thing=34635]Stone Ag[/thing]e, Alien Frontiers treats dice as more than a random sum generator.  For the majority of the actions one can select, the numbers shown on the dice are irrelevant or only marginally relevant; what is important is the relationship between dice.  Some actions require two or three matching dice, some require three sequential dice, and some require one or more dice of any number.  High rolls are far from essential.

Although Alien Frontiers’ innovation on the dice-placement system would seem to be beneficial to mitigate dice randomness, it guts from the game much of what is appealing about the worker placement system: strategic blocking.  By tying some of the best spots on the board to particularly rare combinations of die rolls, Alien Frontiers replaces player competition for scarce spaces with a random constraint on those spaces.  The system actually hampers your ability to plan around another player’s wants and needs, so you end up playing a much more solitary game.

The solitaire feeling of the game is further exacerbated by the fact that many of the more accessible core board spots are so balanced that using any one of them rarely appeared sub-optimal.  Consequently the allocation decisions felt relatively meaningless.  (For what it's worth, my wife's opinion was, "I'm not sure that I liked it, but it was nice not having to think.").  

The game introduces Alien Tech cards that grant players special powers (usually involving beneficial dice or resource manipulation) in an attempt to generate some player stratification.  This is the tableau I was referring to.  Compared to the spaces on the board, these cards seem very desirable (similar to the cards in Stone Age that everyone underestimates until the end of the game).  The powers are cheap enough to use that players will often want to activate several cards on a single turn.  But the more Alien Tech cards players collect, the harder they are to keep track of (and they are extremely text-heavy), which meant the worker placement aspect becomes further marginalized.  If I don't know what my opponent can do, then I don't know what they'll want to do, so I can't plan to block them even if I have the dice, so the best I can do is just take what I want most and hope it interferes with them somehow.   The designer has managed to turn worker placement into something with the solitary feel of Dominion.

The upside to the lack of decision space is that we didn't face any downtime issues until the very end of the game. It was just roll, place, roll, place, etc.  With more players, or the wrong group, I could see AP being a problem, though.  Especially with a large tableau of Tech cards allowing you to flip this die, add one to that die, steal this card, use that card, etc.

The goal of all this dice rolling and placement is purportedly to settle colonies to control areas on some distant moon/planet.   However, the way the points are scored (1 point per colony, 1 point for a majority in an area), two-player area control is a zero sum proposition and thus completely uninteresting.  There is too much board space and no incentive to ever have more than a bare majority of colonies in an area.  The game tries to rectify this with powers for controlling certain areas, but, like many of the core board spaces, they kind of felt interchangeable.

The way the board is balanced, I can't see any player discovering a strategy to take a big lead in colonizing, at least with just two players.  Since the game ends when a player places their last colony, the first person to do so in a two-player game should, by default, win by one or two points.  It seems like this should be the starting player more often than not.  (And that one energy token for going second isn’t really much of an offset.)  There are two Tech cards, which may or may not come out during the game, that each give 1 VP.  If the second player manages to snag both of those, I suppose it could go the other way.  However, something about this whole aspect of the game just seems flat, if not downright broken. (We under-utilized the pirating space because it was late and we didn't feel like being ruthless on a first play of a new game, so maybe that's the key to fixing this issue?)  After playing [thing=62219]Dominant Species[/thing] this month, I would have loved to see other ways of benefitting from being in a region, even when you don’t have an outright majority. 

This may all sound negative, but I’m not ready to give up on Alien Frontiers yet.  My very preliminary opinion is that Alien Frontiers is fine, but likely too shallow to be interesting (at this point, I would lump it in with games like [thing=40692]Small World[/thing] and Stone Age).  It’s the sort of game that appeals to people who, like my wife, find it nice not to think sometimes.  It’s also the sort of game for people who can get immersed in a theme simply because they like the theme – people who write strategy articles titled “[thread=577673]High Octane Dictkitude[/thread]” or who make explosion noises when activating the plasma cannon.  I’m not one of those people.  

As far as dice games go, I probably prefer it marginally to Stone Age.  I greatly prefer it to Kingsurg.  I bought it because there are some dice-lovers in my current game group and I can only play Catan and Stone Age so often.  Even with all its flaws, it will marginally improve my game day experience.  

I also want to thank [user=CleverMojo][/user]
The service was truly great, and the quality of production is fantastic for a first game.  Even though the gameplay hasn’t yet resonated with me, it’s a game I’m proud to have on my shelf.  

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5.5
The game is ok. It starts off fun, but it often over stays its welcome. I am close to hating the experience by the end of each rare play.

There are two problems:
1) It lends itself to a lot of AP. As there are a lot of ways to manipulate dice/resources, a player can go about things in multiple ways. Since a player can't plan their move before their turn, it becomes very boring.

2) The Raider's Outpost can lend itself to a runaway leader problem. The first person to get additional ships can use the outpost to keep other players struggling while slowly moving themselves forward. I am tempted to take a black marker to it.

RATING: 9
This is a great game.  There is not as much randomness as one might think regarding a dice driven resource game.   There are so many options on player turns that just about any roll can advance your game.

This game plays just as well with two, three, or four players, BUT the plethora of turn choices will drive those prone to analysis paralysis nuts, and the game can drag while said AP person takes a turn.  

I have threatened to introduce a turn timer to speed the game along, although I haven't tried it yet.

Nevertheless, a fantastic game.  One of our favorite as a two-player.

So much fun, but probably keep it to 2-3 players.

RATING: 8
You lead a corporation colonizing a new planet.  To do so, you'll need to mine the nearby asteroid belt, gather some solar rays from the nearby satellite array and occasionally crash a spaceship into the planet and tell its inhabitants to suck it up, plant your flag and start farming potatoes.

Interesting Mechanic: Aggressive Dice Placement.  Alien Frontiers is a dice placement game, similar to Kingsburg, which is coming. =)  However, this game varies in Kingsburg in a couple of ways.  The first is that it looks more at patterns of dice - straights, or three-of-a-kinds for examples, which makes more dice viable on a given turn.  But the second is more aggressive.  There are only about 8 locations to place your dice, but multiple people can place their dice there.  However, there are a limited number of slots, and the dice rolls may block your opponents -- you can only mine if the die you are placing is equal to or higher than all other dice already placed.  

This core mechanic makes for a quick-moving resource management game that still has a lot of player interaction, which is to say, don't hoard your resources, or they won't be yours for very long.

RATING: 3
Components: Attractive board, with mostly sensible icons on the various space platforms. The artwork on the cards is great. I wouldn't mind dice that are somehow also shaped like spaceships.

Experience: Hmm. At first, I was loving it. All the dice-rolling, always something interesting to do with my dice. But after ninety minutes of slowly carving out a place for myself in this alien world, I became weary of how easy it was for my opponents to hose me. Capricious dice are one thing, but opponents toying with my fate is another. 

Now, after a second play, it's not the capriciousness that bothers me, but the looong downtime between turns. Assuming each of my three opponents takes just 2 minutes to play out their turn (optimistic), I'll be sitting with nothing to do for six minutes at a stretch, with no way to plan my next move. That's way too long.

It has such a great central mechanism, but two hours (for the shorter game) is just too much, with too much empty space, for me to want to play this again.

First Played: 2012-03-06
Last Played: 2013-01-22
Eagerness to Play Again: No Desire.

Plays (2):
4 players: 2

RATING: 9.2
As a playtester who works with the game designer, Tory, I get to play this game at least once a week. We have a blast and, for the last few weeks, we have been playing the final production copy Tory received. 

As a supporter on Kickstarter I have a copy on preorder. I am very impressed how the original painted artwork, the professional icon graphics and the solid colored dice manage to come across without clashing or distracting the player. The painted artwork is in mostly dark and muted colors. The icons and text are bright colors with sharp edges so they float above the realistic artwork. Finally. The solid colored dice are large and in colors that stand out so they are easy to locate at the beginning of your turn when you need to gather them and roll. 

We meet to play games almost every lunch hour during the week and AF gets played about 2-3 times a week. 

I cannot wait for my copy to play it with my family!

[2015-02-26 Rating: 7 -> 6]
After playing Alien Frontiers a few more times, I have to adjust my rating. Just as I thought, the direct negative player interaction that is featured in this game is not really my cup of tea. It's not so bad that I wouldn't play Alien Frontiers again, but given the choice, I'd always prefer something else.

[2012-10-25 Rating: n.a. -> 7]
Alien Frontiers (AF) was a game that was on my wish list for quite some time. What kept me from buying it were the comments about leader-bashing and king-making. I don't like my games to be decided this way. So I stayed away from it. Lately, I got the chance to try AF (2 players). In this configuration, I really enjoyed the game. 

There are quite a few ways to use what you've rolled and with the help of some Alien Tech cards, your options do increase in later rounds. You even have that fine line between scoring and advancing your engine. If you get distracted by all the shiny Tech cards, you might be too far behind in the race for points to win. If you underdevelop your engine you might be stuck with what you rolled and lose a turn which is bad. Balacing these two factors needs makes your turns and the game fun.

But there are also some things that I don't like that much. As I read, the screwage factor is quite high in AF and I guess kingmaking will become a problem with more than two players. So, my rating is for AF with two players only until I've tried it with more players.

Another thing to keep in mind: This is a dice game. So there's certainly some luck involved. Rolling doubles in the beginning is great and you might get behind if you can't get the dice you need to start your engine. If you're looking for a game in which you can formulate a plan and execute it, stay away from Alien Frontiers. This might get even worse with more players as I anticipate a lot of player induced chaos on top of the luck of the roll.

RATING: 6
Alien Frontiers is an Worker Placement game / Area Majority game (reminds me of Belfort in this respect).  Dice are rolled to determine your legal plays each turn.  There are some interesting synergies and decisions to be made regarding when to "purchase" extra dice, and when to discard them in lieu of points.  There is also an interesting interplay with getting resources which could get stolen, or moving forward in a sure manner.  All in all, this is a fairly tight game, but it's not something I enjoy playing that much.  I'm not sure why.  Somehow the theme doesn't come through for me.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Players are colonists orbiting a distant alien planet, trying to colonize it.  Players on their turn roll their ships (dice) and allocate them out to tasks for the turn. Tasks include making new ships (more dice), gathering resources (ore and fuel), and founding colonies on the planet. It is all livened up by some chaotic alien powers which add a substantial take that element to the game.  The player with the most victory points (via colonies and majorities on the planet) wins the game.  It moves along fairly quickly, although can slow down with deliberate, careful players.  A lot of similarities to games we've seen before, but solidly developed and plays well.  Good presentation, nice game, but just no sparkle for me - I'd rather play Alea Iacta Est or Kingsburg, which feature a lot of the same stuff but in faster, more interactive fashion.   Alien Frontiers seems pre-destined to close finishes with the winner getting up by pure luck ... as if the previous 90 minutes didn't matter.

RATING: 8
solid game, intuitive to learn, and nice colours and components. It makes a great gateway game if you want something a little more challenging than Settlers, as the decisions are often more difficult. There is a lot of bashing the leader though, and the end game can be anti-climactic as it often comes down to who gets the right roll at the right time to end the game. But then again there is a fair amount of skill involved as more experienced players seem to have an advantage, and the more I've played this, the more tricky end game moves I've seen with cards and switching control of territories. It will still be seeing lots of play I'm sure.

RATING: 7.5
A light game of space colonization. Nice mounted map full of graphics accompanied by plenty of colored dice (one color for each player) and alien tech cards. The rules are very easy to digest, leave no ambiguities and game mechanics are simple as well. Each player tries quickly to place all of his colonies on the surface of the planet. First player to achieve this, play ends and each player scores his points to have a winner. It is a smooth fast paced game. The dice of each player are considered to be his space ships. Based on the results of each dice roll the player chooses what action will his spaceships under take. There is an abundance of choices: gather solar power, mine ore, construct and base colonies, raid others’ players resources, use the space market exchange or acquire alien tech cards to help you reach your goal. Lady Luck has her say all the time BUT the way you are going to use your dice/spaceships makes all the difference. Even an unlucky roll can give you benefits because of the very many options a player has, not including the Alien Tech cards which can alter the course of the game and the winner, significantly. After several plays the core game can become bland and repetitive. Adding one or more of the expansions or add-on can change all this and give more flavor to the game.

RATING: 5
Some innovative ideas that don't work all that well in practice.   The game is slow, there's lots of downtime, and you really can't plan ahead due to the (innovative) dice blocking.   Alien Tech cards are very swingy and powerful, so you cannot ignore them.  Suffers from leader-bashing also, since the scoring is open and it is very easy to take points away from people.   So, there's some good ideas here, but it's an unpolished and under-developed game.

RATING: 8
While a lighter worker-placement in my opinion, there's so much fun to be had in making use of workers as dice. The variety of spots makes almost any roll worthwhile, whether you're grabbing a ton of energy, blocking people off on the moon or just settling for some colony advancement.

I've had others complain that they were disadvantaged by poor dice rolling - as with any game with dice, you've got to not take the game so seriously. Even if this is a massive problem for you, pick up some Artifacts, giving you a lot more control over your dice faces.

RATING: 7.5
Véletlenül kipróbálva :-). Vagyis h Zs játszotta volna, de folyamatosan elhívták, már a kezdet kezdete előtt, és h a többiek ne várjanak beszálltam az 1-2 körre, amjd látva mit csinálok, azt mondta, ha már elb*-tam, akkor fejezzem is be. :-)
Komolyabban: na nem azért nem vetet vissza, rendes volt csak. 
Könnyedebb is lehetne, de nem annak szánták, jó ötletek, nekem picit hosszú volt. Illetve hát a kocka szerencse: az utolsó 3 körben egy jó dobással befejezhetem volna a játékot, s még tán meg is nyerem. Ehhez képest a dobások nem úgy sikerültek, lassan csúsztam vissza, végül így utolsó lettem.
Nem nagyon, de a pontok itt a nem nagyon kategória: a végeredmény: 7-6-6-5 pontok voltak. /Vagy 8-7-6-5?, mindegy: kevés pont/
Jól összerakott amúgy, jó választási és döntési lehetőségekkel, egyes területek megszerzése egyben egyes akciók -az adott terület melletti- megsegítése is: kevesebbért ugyanazt, vagy ugyanannyiért többet kap az ember, ha a területet uralja.
A rossz dobásokra is gondoltak: van egy szemetes hely, ami lassan telik meg de legalább van hova tenni a kockákat, és halad is vele az ember. Szóval aranyos játék, de nyelvfüggő, -bár ha nem használod a kártyákat akkor nem :-), ugyanakkor a kártyák azért nem rosszak. Igaz aki nyert sem vett rengeteg kártyát, de az az 1-1- lap is csodákat tehet.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 2
The game’s retro artwork was very attractive, but its retro gameplay was not. I’ve seen people comparing this to Kingsburg which I don’t understand other than they both have dice.  See, in Kingsburg, you place a die/dice, then another player, etc., until everyone has placed all their dice. Here, you place all your dice all at once. This means a player takes their entire turn at once while everyone else waits. 

For me, this game felt a lot more like Settlers of Catan than either KB or Stone Age. Just like in Settlers, people don’t know what the board will be like before it’s their turn, and they very well may have resources stolen from them during other players’ turns, so planning for most people begins at the beginning of their turn. While they decide what to do with their dice, you wait. In a 4P game as people get more of their ships (dice) into the game, the downtime increases and the game drags.

RATING: 8
Not so great with two — the dummy mechanism, doesn't hold a candle to live players. At four players, the game is taunt and interesting; lots of meaty decisions to make. The game passes through very distinct stages as play progresses, having  discernible beginning, middle, and end strategies. We all sensed the depth here, ripe for the exploration of future plays. A great game, far more interesting and stressful than Kingsburg. 

Actually, I think the game has more in common with Stone Age than Kingsburg. 

I was afraid that the game would lack tension, but my concerns were unfounded. While I'm sure that surface players, shallow thinkers, and other forms of neanderthals could drag the game down, making it a chore, with the right players it's a real gem.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
B&B/2011

Similar to other games that assign dice according to the numbers rolled, such Kingsburg, Alea Iacta Est, Troyes. 
Usually you have enough choices, and sometimes hard choices, to place your dice.  Nevertheless, it's an area control dice game that depends on good rolls.

Extremely well designed board, and attractive too, with nice components.  Simple rules & well-written rule book.

Game faster in the beginning, but can really bog down near the end when players have acquired more cards and bonuses. Cannot plan ahead because of a lot of stealing, and placement depends on your roll.

Board easily adapts to 2-3 player games.  In 3-4 player games expect a lot of leader bashing.

Simple strategy involves trying to get more actions. 

It is so simple to teach and to remember how to play (certainly helped by the great board layout), I think this will be an easy one to pull out, especially  when new players are present.

I am lukewarm about the dice, and I do prefer games where players can plan ahead so they can actually play when it is their turn.  When it is my turn and I throw the dice I tend to spend less time than I should considering options, because I don't like people waiting on me.

For what this game is, it needs to be shorter:
*Maybe give each player fewer colonies since the game ends when the last colony has been played.  Since that would reduce conflict, some territories should be randomly excluded.  
*Maybe limiting a player to two cards--a lot of time was spent reading cards that could be stolen, reading your own & trying to decide the order of play, and reading the 3 available cards.  
*Maybe only two cards (instead of 3) face up for purchase.  Would reduce the reading time.

If it were a shorter game, my rating would be higher.

RATING: 8
Alien Frontiers turned out to be a very pleasant surprise. I'd heard the comparisons to Troyes, a game I've owned for a while now, and was always glad I had it instead, being as it's touted as the more "gamer's game" of the two. But Alien Frontiers turned out to be a lot more different from Troyes than I was expecting.  Area majority plays a huge part (one of my favorite mechanisms), and while having a lighter feel, it almost seems like there are more options on how to use one's dice in this one, especially when playing with the Factions expansion which we also tried this month. The artwork is great, the rules are pretty straightforward, and there are usually at least a few good options every turn.  It's a solid game, and I'm glad that I have both it and Troyes in our collection.

RATING: 6.5
Érdekes játék, még nem játszottam hasonló mechanizmussal. Kicsit olyan mintha a kockapóker találkozna worker placementtel. 
Valamiért a scifi játékok hangulatukban engem nem nagyon tudnak megfogni, így volt ez ezzel is, de a mechanizumas alapjaiban nagyon tetszett.
Az első játék gyakorlatilag sötétben tapogatózásssal telt. Ami lejött belőle: 
- nagyon nagy a kitolási faktor a játékba, és sajnos nekem erősen kingmaker szagú is.
- a szerencse szerepét képtelenség megbecsülni benne, mivel annyira el van rejtve. Talán nincs is benne olyan sok szerepe, szóval ez inkébb pozitívum.
- ahogy az embernek egyre több hajója/területe/kártyája lesz hatványozottan nő a kombinációk száma. Így rengeteg idő megy el a gondolkodással sakkozással, ami miatt nagyon sok a holtidő. A majdani dobásod ismerete nélkül előre gondolkodni nincs sok értelme, így mindenki uncsizik, amíg rá nem kerül a sor. Viszont a dobás után a nyakadba szakad kb. 100 kijátszható kombináció, amin természetesen még a leggyorsabb játékos is 3-5 percet gondolkodik.

Szívesen játszok még vele, mert ezzel a játékkal egy játék nem játék. Azért azt elsőre is éreztem, hogy nem ez lesz a kedvencem. 

Kíváncsi lennék rá, hogy 50-100 játékos veteránoknak mennyi ideig tart egy kör a végjátékban.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Alien Frontiers does many things right, but a few things wrong in ways that overshadow the rest of the design.

The basic dice management game works fine, although it is fairly one-dimensional because the only way to score points is to get colonies down onto the surface. The downtime for such a simple system, however, is excruciating. There is very little planning ahead because the situation can (and will) change completely until it's your turn. Docking ports will be occupied, your resources and cards will be stolen, your ships blasted from space, and of course there is the dice factor, too. This problem gets even worse as the game progresses because of the many technologies and bonuses that influence the game state (and I actually like many of the technologies).

Probably even worse than the downtime problem is the leader bashing, however. Alien Frontiers allows direct targeting of specific players, e.g. by stealing resources from them or destroying their property. This works as a great equalizer if one players sticks his head out too far. Everyone else will simply target that player until he has been pulled back into the fold. I'd rather have a run-away leader problem.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
A fantastic blend of dice allocation, resource management and area control mechanics with inspired retro Sci-Fi art direction. A little bit euro, a little bit "take that" Ameritrash, which has good and bad points. The transparency of the publisher and their KickStarter story is very interesting too. Can suffer from Analysis Paralysis at the endgame [though hidden agenda cards from expansion helps this to some degree IMHO] and the Raiders Outpost makes this very confrontational, and there is a kingmaker issue occasionally, but the game is definitely FUN and one that gets alot more table time than most of my collection as a result. I teach this to non-boardgame-playing gamers alot and it goes over well.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I've had this one on my shelf for a while and I'm glad I finally got it on the table to play. Initially the game can feel a bit daunting with the many symbols on the board, but the game is quickly understood minutes in the first play through. Alien Frontiers shines in the fact that is offers the player many different decisions. Do I try to block my opponent's ships or do I want to gain another ship to be able to place next turn? While luck is still an important factor in Alien Frontiers, players must rely on their tactical decisions be able to establish colonies and receive victory points.

The largest issue with Worker Placement games is that players can feel like they are playing competing solitaire games. Alien Frontiers has the perfect amount of player interaction. The game is mostly focused on building up competitively rather than attempting to destroy your opponent. A close game can be satisfying for the winners and losers since it's not too cutthroat (though the raiders outpost can be an annoying tactic).

Overall, I'd definitely recommend Alien Frontiers.  
Pros:
- Easy to pick up
- Solid gameplay mechanics
- Fitting spaceship colonization theme
- Constant tatical decisions
- Ideal amount of player interaction

Negative:
- Generic game pieces
- Basic, but not necessarily poor art

RATING: 8
Call it what it is...Yahtzee in space. And it's awesome. Ok...it's more than that...plenty of options and card combos to mitigate the dice - and plenty of "take that!", so it's not multiplayer solitaire (like Yahtzee). 

You can't play nice in this game either - well, if you do, then whomever gets more dice will usually win. You need to constantly be removing dice from the board (plasma cannon), and eliminating the usage of territory powers (Isolation Field Generator) - and seeing how moving colonies can cause other players to lose points and powers! 

You can't be afraid to use the "discard power" of the cards. Use 'em!

Plays quickly (can be prone to AP, but this tends to go away after a few plays) - great game!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
It has dice rolls, and plenty of them, so the luck factor is there.  But the game is effective in what you do with what you got, and the game is, interestingly enough, balanced in terms of the luck factor each player gets.  But at the same time, it is possible to get some terrible dice rolls that don't give you as many opportunities as you need.  But from my experience, it's not a game breaker.  This is one of the few games that manages the luck factor in a nice controlled way.  It's worth at least 1 play to see how you would enjoy it.  In terms of luck vs. skill, I would go for about 60-40 in favor of luck, which is quite an accomplishment for a game relying on dice rolls for gameplay.  I guess it's because the gameplay is fun, and there's many ways to win which allows you do adapt to any dice roll you get.  It's not perfect, but it's still fun.

RATING: 5
Impressive physical production, both attractive and ergonomic. I like the cards, which allow for some interesting tricks in an otherwise straightforward game.

My principal criticism of Alien Frontiers is that it moves too slowly and lasts too long. To justify its current pace and length, there would need to be more stuff to do, and there would need to be a more interesting game arc from beginning to middle to end. The downtime is especially annoying because you can't plan anything until your turn begins. Other games in this genre solve this problem by letting you roll your dice in advance (Kingsburg, Troyes), or by allowing you to do only one thing at a time (Alea Iacta Est, Airships, Kingsburg, Troyes). The turn structure of Alien Frontiers is a real weakness when compared to these competitors.

RATING: 2.1
I was convinced I like this game, but:
Noticable downtime in a four player game, almost over the threshold of "I got bored waiting" as you cannot prepare your turn.
My impression of the game was mostly influenced by the following: there are "big" dice games where you can mitigate poor dice rolls (for example Kingsburg or even Bora Bora). In AF however not. In the beginning of the game you need doubles to get you more dice. If this is not possible you´re screwed. Alternatively you will get resources instead. A lot of resources makes it probable that they will be stolen, which is another remarkable set back. Both happened to me and while I was still on 3 dices other players already got the max amount of 6. Poor dice rolls and being victim of the Raiders = horribly unbalanced game. Even when I rolled a double then the shipyard was occupied with other players dice...
One should not be allowed to steal 4 resources of the same player. Nor should it be possible to steal the only alien tech card one possess. Rather this ability should be bought first (in form of an Alien Tech card, so it comes into play but later) instead of being allowed right from the start.
Even if a fourth player gets two resources as compensation of the disadvantage I didn´t get use of it because of being robbed. So the only verdict I can decide on this game (out of the box) is broken. Maybe it would work with "kind" fellow players, but they aren´t.
It has a cool theme and lots of nice ideas but the overpowered raiders outpost ruins the game.

RATING: 7
For the record, dice and I are not friends. But, that does not influence my rating or review of this game. I approach AF with the same sort of masochistic foreboding that I do with other dice centric games. That is, prepared for doom on any given turn. However, AF is more like "Roll Through The Ages" in that 1) the dice can be used for multiple options not just a high/low sum & 2) the dice can be (legally) manipulated - in AF by using some alien tech card powers. This tends to moderate the impact of the actual dice rolls... but not completely... it does still have a bit of luck factor and if one gets behind in the colony placement race I've found it fairly hard to catch up in time. Still an enjoyable game when in the right mood. And I think it is susceptible to the usual balancing issues in 3 player games, so 4 or 2 are better. More of a building race with 2, more of a "king of the hill" with 4.
(Note, I have not recorded many of my plays of this game, as usual for me with any game.)

RATING: 9
Many gamers discuss how Alien Frontiers compares to Kingsburg and which is the better game.  For me this is not the question: I love Kingsburg.  I love Stone Age and I love Alien Frontiers.  All three games use dice rolling, worker placement and resource collection, however with different focus.   Kingsburgs main focus is risk management because of the threat cards.  Stone Age puts the focus on it different pathes to victory points.  Both are typical indirect Euro-style games.  Alien Frontiers shares some mechanics with its older brothers but offers more interactivity and is slightly more aggressive without becoming frustrating.  I like how different combinations of dice can be used and how the dice go together with the alien tech cards.  Overall gameplay is quite smooth, and not as AP-prone as I feared.  Very recommended as advanced family game or light gamers game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Very good game in the vein of "Kingsburg", but it feels a little heavier, and combinations of dice are more important than high numbers. Instead of constructing buildings with different abilities, you just get technology cards and some majority bonuses on the board, while victory points come from placing colonies.

There is a continuous game flow (instead of game rounds), where you do every step of your turn at once. That means you have no possibility of planning during other players' turns, as you must wait for yours to roll your dice and see which spaces are blocked, and that increases downtime. The low and tight scores would fit better in a system of the race type, either first to place all colonies or first to reach a certain score. I'm not fond of the "take-that" elements, either. Those may be very strong, particularly in a two-player game, and may favor bashing the leader or kingmaking with more players.

However, the game still has a lot of interesting choices. There are cards that allow you to manipulate the dice, reducing luck. The Sci-Fi theme is always a big plus with me, and the artwork is good enough.

RATING: 9
Terrific game, that rare gem that is both fast, fun and engaging.  It is a dice game, and the way the dice are used is such that no matter what you roll, you will have several choices available.

I only have two caveats (thus a 9 and not a 10).  One is that if you do not get a spaceship early in the game, you are not going to win, and it is a matter of some luck when you get your first spaceship.  It is still fun to play, even if you are not winning, though.  The other caveat is that it can be bothersome to play with someone subject to analysis paralysis, as there are so many combinations for each roll.  Especially later in the game when there are more spaceships.


=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Much like Kingsburg, Alien Frontiers has players rolling dice and then using them as "workers" which are placed on the board in different places for different benefits. The board does a good job of providing attractive options regardless of what you roll, but of-a-kinds, straights, etc. are often more beneficial. 

On the face of it, Alien Frontiers suffers from the same inherent problem that Kingsburg does: you can't really develop a strategy. You have to deal with the dice you roll. If you want to develop more ships quickly, for example, you may not be able to; you are at the mercy of the roll. And yet to eliminate this aspect would be to make every roll feel exactly the same, in which case you might as well be using workers instead of dice. The answer is the Alien Tech cards, which provide a way to spend minor amounts of resources and modify the dice in favorable ways. This is a good solution, and creates some tough choices about whether to spend resources for a slightly better roll, or take the slightly worse one and save resources for later. 

The early game is all about developing an engine, while the endgame turns into fighting to defend your colonies and cards from the other players. I like this highly interactive endgame, but it may catch some people by surprise in comparison to how the game starts out. 

Unfortunately, this endgame tug-of-war can have some kingmaker problems. The hidden goals (potential victory points) in the expansion would seem to fix this nicely, as it is otherwise a bit too calculable. Since each player has the power to end the game as it nears the end, there should be some incentive for players not to take the lead and immediately end the game. The hidden goals provide this incentive. Since the expansion also adds a 5th player, it seems like a must-have addition. It's a long game considering how samey each turn can be, but the overall arc of the game from development to combat is nicely realized. A solid game that jumps from a 7 to an 8 when played with the expansion.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
After reading the rules, thought it was similar to KINGSBURG, but this is NOT the case, as rolling higher numbers does not ensure you get better resources.

As player dice STAY ON THE BOARD between turns, it is possible to partially block, or affect the "cost" of playing a certain action.

Since what you rolled limits your available choices of actions, it makes it difficult to plan ahead, as all you can do is choose a general direction and hope things don't change too much until your turn comes around again.

Still, it is next to impossible to be blocked, as there are tech cards which have various powers that you can use, which allows you to bend certain rules, modify the die results, re-roll, etc.

I like the fact that you can make the game longer by playing with 8 Colony tokens per players.  My group found 6 tokens was just a bit too long, but after a few more plays, it should clock in the listed time.

Further, there is a 5th player expansion, which I may not pick up as it would make the game too long.  But the option will be there for those who prefer 5-player games and/or have more time to play.

All in all, a nice design which leaves enough decision making, where players are not cornered, but where there is still a good deal of luck (die rolling).  As I mentioned, this can be mitigated with the right cards and card combos!

The area majority on the planet, which gives you points and a special card/power if you have a majority, is simple, and seems to work fine.

The only negative aspect is the rulebook, which is missing a few important cross-references, and where some of the important rules should have been listed in bold and/or highlighted, so they could be easily referenced.

As an example, where it says that your dice stay on the table between turns, perhaps a little highlighting might have shown this to be a key rule of the game.

The Data Crystal exception regarding the Burroughs Desert should also have been listed under the Burroughs Desert section, else people would think that the Alien die will keep changing hands.  Actually, this puts more importance on the territory at the center of the board, where I would expect folks try to prevent a majority.

Looking forward to trying this again, now that we seem to have figured out all the fiddly details we missed on our first go.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Considering that this was a Kickstarter, I'd say that this is a huge success.

RATING: 8.5
The gameplay is interactive and at times mean. Making good use of the dice/ships means often skipping over something you'd really like to do in favor of trying to mitigate the behavior of other players.

The end of the game can come very quickly, so the tension ratchets up after the midgame on each turn.

My gripe about the game is the publisher and how expensive the game is. The game and its expansions seem to be always teetering on the edge of out of print. Expansions are nearly the price of a new game from a different publisher. Early versions of the game had misprints. Still to this point you cannot determine based on the publisher's info whether the 4th Edition, 2nd printing comes with the plastic domes or the wooden domes. Smaller publishers always seem to enjoy making the act of buying their game a puzzle to solve.

I would never play with more than 4 players. The rocket dice option is ridiculous and badly executed, do not invest in them regardless of how much you like the game.

I love the gameplay and I love the artwork. There's rich strategy and strong theme included. The game is a perfect amount of fun for 90-120 minutes of play. A comprehensive FAQ from the publisher with descriptions of all of the tech and agenda cards would be welcomed.
