=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
The artwork is good, there is an interesting assortment of actions to choose from.  Each player is competing to be the dominant faction colonizing an alien world.  It is a funky Euro game with a significant amount of "take that" in it.  Dice are rolled at the beginning of each player's turn and allocated to select actions.    There is a strong area majority control element and the current player with a majority in a particular region gets to benefit from a very useful bonus which is different from the other bonuses available in other regions.  These bonuses usually provide some kind of a resource discount when performing future actions.  There is a very fun deck of alien technology cards which can be obtained in order to bend the rules in manifold interesting ways.  Alien Frontiers is an excellent game and it's fun too.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Easy to see why there are mixed reviews. On the one hand, you have the coolness factor, nice graphics and some good ideas in the game, on the other problems....

So the positive is I like what the game is trying to do and some of the ideas in the game.  I like the cards and how they let you mitigate the dice, dice allocation worked well mostly and gave you some choices, area influence was a little simple but okay, graphics were good.  

For the issues, let's start with the big two, length and downtime, which are the worst offenders IMO. Probably a light middle-weight at most so 2 - 2.5 is just too long, by about 45 minutes, 90 minutes at most would have been a decent length for this. The downtime, bad bad bad as the dice increase AND you can't plan buggerall which is the worst kind of downtime, really each player doing one action would have been so much better than one player doing his/her turn while everyone waits, then the next player goes... 

Then there are the other things; Possible kingmaking, definite leader bashing, rolling doubles too important early on, games will likely always be very close so luck will be a factor in who wins. Then you have the "6" space for colonizing that can be effectively blocked for a few rounds near the end of the game by one player, this is a no-no. The stealing spot is too cheap and fruitful which means lots of screwage to go around so if you had any little plan at all, now you don't (maybe 4 dice would balance this a little more). Finally, decisions are somewhat made for you by the dice; depending on what you roll, this dictates what you can do and usually the best options are somewhat obvious like getting ships early and colonize asap as much as possible after.

Bottom line for me is for this kind of game I definitely prefer Kingsburg (with exp) for now.
 
Possible houserules - Yes the game works as is but many think it could be improved. The obvious thing is to have people do one action only, then the next player, etc. Unfortunatly this might require a few more changes to work properly, not quite sure though. The length could be tweaked too probably though then you would really have an empty planet, again another change or two just to make this work...combined with the other issues not sure all the effort would be worth it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
Alien Frontiers takes the dice rolling mechanics of Castles of Burgundy and pushes them even further, requiring more Yahtzee-like combinations of dice to perform certain actions and introducing some worker placement-like blocking. The game has a number of interesting decisions to make and provides several paths to victory. It's enjoyable to puzzle out the best way to allocate dice.

However, the game has two serious flaws that ruin it for me. The first is the inclusion of aggressive attacking (beyond the indirect attacking in the area control portion of the game). It feels out of place in a strategic game like this and can turn the game into one where players have to pretend they are doing well to avoid attacks. The bigger issue is the core mechanic of rolling dice. Bad dice rolls can cost players games. There is always something one can do in this game, but certain dice rolls can provide significantly better options. There's also no way to manipulate dice rolls without investing in special abilities. On top of all this, the inability to roll dice before a player's turn slows the game down (due to no pre-turn planning).

The bad outweighs the good for me, so Alien Frontier is a game I tend to avoid.

RATING: 9
- One of my personal favourites.
- I love the puzzle of how to best utilise the cards, territory bonuses and player abilities to execute your strategy.
- The way the action spaces use different combinations of dice, combined with the card abilities means that most dice rolls present good options. Whether the dice enable what you originally wanted to do is the question, but there are plenty of ways to manipulate the dice with the cards if you're a player that prefers more control.
- I also like the way players' dice remain on spaces until the start of their next turn. This adds an additional layer of strategy and contributes to the player interaction as you're always blocking/being blocked.
- The game looks great on the table, plays okay with all player counts, however I think 3 is the best, followed closely by 4.
- I recommend the factions expansion though I don't own any of the others. I like the goals and the player powers in factions, but I feel the other exp. will just add additional unnecessary mechanics that won't be worth the extra rules explanation.
- Teaching this is okay, but there is a fair amount to take in for players looking for the next step after Catan (for example).

RATING: 8
A game in which the pieces are the 2-7 dice you roll, and the dice you roll determine what those pieces can do: increase the number of dice, sacrifice a 6 to build a settlement, triples build a settlement, get resources, steal resources, etc. There are plenty of different strategies and the pieces are well made. A fairly original game mechanic that keeps it open and encourages repeated plays.

BIG PLUS: easy to learn, great graphics, dice rolling
BIG MINUS: limited slots may mean the right roll comes too late

QUIBBLE MINUS: there are multiple editions, with each one getting more intricate pieces… and so conversion kits are available, when they shouldn't be
EVEN BIGGER MISS: this is one game that most retailers are not allowed to ship… so buying it online is a pain

RATING: 6

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
The game is really quite simple. There are a number of action fields on the board that require poker-like dice combos to activate (pair, three-of-a-kind, straight) and other fields that take dice of any value or give things depending on the value. There is always something to do with your dice, but you can't always do what you want. Dice placed by other players can block you from certain actions and you can't be sure if your desired actions will be blocked or if you'll have the values to use them until you actually roll your dice. The object is to place colonies on the central board in an area majority game that generates points and special abilities. You can also acquire cards to enable you to manipulate your rolls and move dice and colonies among other actions.

I do enjoy some of the more creative dice-manipulation games, but for my tastes, this one is too much dependent on your rolls and there is little to think about between turns because so much can change. The game can be almost unbearable with a slow player at the table. This is a fairly light, quick game and needs to be played as such to be enjoyable. King-making and bash-the-leader are also symptoms of the game and are additional reasons to take the game lightly.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

The dice only seem to add a small amount of randomness because there always seems to be something good to do with them.

RATING: 6
I purchased Alien Frontiers because of the buzz and positive reviews from almost everyone that posted on BGG after AF's release. Worker placement games are one of my favorite genres and I probably played the majority of the well known games, including Agricola, Le Havre, Caylus, Stone Age, Age of Empires III. But I haven't played Kingsburg, so I was very curious to see how a mechanism that involved dice rolling was implemented in such a category of games. I was pleasantly suprised. My expectations were extremely high for this game, and it didn't live up to them. But my first and only game so far was really good, and the randomness that comes with dice didn't stand in the way of controling my gameplay. I'm very curious to see how this game will develop in my opinion. UPDATE: after 3 plays I can say that it didn't develop very well, I like it less than I hoped I would. The main reason: you can only start thinking after you've rolled the dice, and that slows the game down immensely.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

However, there's still something about the game that doesn't feel right to me. But having said that, having picked it up again later, I'm more intrigued to play it again and determine what the element is that isn't hitting home.

RATING: 4
I was disappointed by this game.  I can see why people would like it, as for many people the SF theme is appealing.  I don't care for the theme, but my problem with the game lies in the actual gameplay rather than the theme.  

There have been a lot of "use dice as action" games and I don't think that this makes any improvement on any of those.  I dislike Kingsburg, enjoy Yspahan, and I think Livingstone and Troyes are not bad.

Even though there are ways to mitigate the luck of the dice, luck seems to play a big part.  But I don't have that big of a problem with luck either.  The main problem is that I found the game to lack excitement.  The decisions were not interesting and there was little tension at all.  It also seemed to last a little too long, shaving a half hour off the play time would have made a big difference.

RATING: 7

I found there was rarely nothing I could do and with the outer belt expansion the problem of being too late to a specific action field is mostly mitigated by the chances the belt offers.

I should play this more often :)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.7
After one play, I have to say I like this little game. I like the dice-as-playing-pieces aspect, and the domed cities are adorable. But for a lighter game, it takes a long time. The problem is that nobody can plan moves ahead: the dice are inaccessible until the next turn. A game like [thing=102680][/thing], which is by all standards a weightier game, has faster-moving turns because players can get their mancala pieces into position ahead of time and plan what they will do for this Builder action and what will happen upon activation of that Trajan tile. This gives players something to do when it is not their turns and helps keep the game moving swiftly. Not so in Alien Frontiers. The game would most likely benefit from players being given two sets of dice so that they could roll and plan ahead during downtime. Also some extra player interaction would be nice--for instance, what if instead of blocking everybody out of the get-a-new-die spot until a player's next turn, that player could be bumped off the board and PAID for his trouble in resources, cards, etc.? Blocking other people out of a spot is just not direct enough player interaction for my tastes, and I think it needlessly extends the game. Still, I enjoyed my first play and want to play again.

RATING: 7
This was a game where I wish I had when I didn't, and wanted to play it so bad.  When I got it, the first few plays were really good, but AP is a real dead lock in this game.  I have a pretty AP prone friend and due to the mechanics of this game's pace, it really grind on us.  The game lasted over 5+ hours and it really shouldn't have.  Which lead to subsequent games to be timed turns to cut down on the AP.  Even at 2 mins a turn it dragged on for quite a bit with 3 players.

Theme is amazing, artwork is amazing, overall wonderful game and I don't think I'm getting rid of it ever.  I'd just recommend that you play with people who are less prone to AP and in the first couple of plays, lower the win conditions so you can start a timed game there after.

RATING: 8.4

RATING: 7
Alien frontiers is a gateway / family game to me in the sense that rules are easy to explain and scoring is close.
the game is easy to play as well
and it is just short enough to place itself i those categories.

Thae art is nice. the board is functional and the dice worker mechanism works pretty good. Before alien frontiers Kingsburg did it. Troyes improved on that. And Alien frontiers well it feels different. and is a pretty unique game.
I've played over 500 different games now and while alien frontiers has it plusses like for instance Takenoko it misses an element for me to play with friends that have played over 200 different games. they too see the strong points as a family game but it lacks depth. We have no wish to explore it further.

Having said that Alien frontiers has for now a steady place in my collection and I will propose it  with non gamers and families. My personal rating would be a 6. I would place it next to stone age and Takenoko. that's why I rate it a 7. those games are still fun to play for me and excellent with the right group.

Was One of my favorites. Really clever way to use dice as worker placement. I also enjoy any game that can be so appreciated on a tactical level - a particular turn can be very impressive, so much so that watching other players make interesting moves is part of the fun of the game.  Some negatives: The AP on this can get bad, and it usually takes longer than 90min to play. 1/16/14: I've now decided that this game, with 4+ people, maybe 3 as well, is an exercise in chaos at the end game.  It can still be fun, but with all the interesting tactical elements in the beginning of the game, it's a strange mix and not the thing that attracted me to the game. So I like it more with the 2.

RATING: N/A
Alien Frontiers is a game of resource management and planetary development for two to four players. During the game you will utilize orbital facilities and alien technology to build colony domes in strategic locations to control the newly discovered world.

The game board shows the planet, its moon, the stations in orbit around the planet, and the solar system’s star. The dice you are given at the start of the game represent the space ships in your fleet. You will assign these ships to the orbital facilities in order to earn resources, expand your fleet, and colonize the planet.

Con:

RATING: 6
LIKES:

* Dice stay on the board blocking other players from choosing the action.  Of course, this also effects you!
* Different viable actions available depending on what you roll (IE - 3 die straight, a pair, higher number, etc.).  Also, one spot you can always place dice if you have nowhere else to go.
* Alien tech cards do add some nice flavor/special abilities to the game.  Do NOT let someone dominate these or set up a nice combination.

DISLIKES:
* One really cannot plan ahead due to uncertainty of what will be available on the board and what one will roll.  This can lead to quite a bit of dead time as more deliberate players wrestle out their best possible move.
* This game needs to move quickly to keep my attention due to lack of many options.  If it moves slowly, this game drags for me since you really cannot plan ahead.  

If everyone plays quickly, I rate the game a 6.  If even one person plays more slowly and deliberately, I DON'T want to play and would rate it a 2.

RATING: 8
Alien Frontiers is a new indie dice game about colonizing planets. It combines a handful of standard euro ideas – resource management, worker placement, area control, even using dice as “workers” seems to be the new new thing – into a more or less fresh package. While it is not without issues, it works pretty well.

Since I went to the trouble of coming up with a set of criteria for judging dice games, I may as well apply them here:

1) Downtime is the Enemy: This is the biggest problem with Alien Frontiers. While you only roll your dice once on your turn, there are enough options (plus a small special powers element which broadens the decision tree) that analysis paralysis is a real possibility, especially among players that are unfamiliar with the game. My first game (with four) took almost an hour and a half (with me and another player perhaps being a little too chatty for the flow of play). I’m sure this would come down to an hour or even 45 minutes with experienced players, but that’s a big hurdle to jump over. There’s a little bit to consider while other players are going, and a bit of mild take-that to add player interaction, but downtime is, by and large, downtime.

2) No More Than One Roll per Turn: The biggest win of the design. You roll once each turn and only reroll, maybe, one die thanks to a special power card. Almost enough by itself to make me recommend AF :)

3) Give Players a Chance to React to the Dice: Also a big win, although also the root cause of the AP problems. There are some special powers that offer simple dice manipulations, but the important part of the game here is that you assign dice to different actions, with each action requiring a certain pattern (like a straight or a pair). There is one option that works best with low dice, plus a dump option for miscellaneous dice, as well. As a result, you usually have a couple of plausible options to consider after you roll. This does not mean that there isn’t still a luck of the dice factor, but it’s not out of line.

4) Low Rolls Should Not Suck, High Rolls Should Not Rule: A mixed bag here. Some of the actions have multiple slots available for workers/dice to occupy, but if a player already has dice present, you can only place if your set is higher than theirs. This mechanic adds some indirect interaction and tension, but it also ups the luck factor by making high rolls generally better than low. There is at least one action that works better with low dice, but it’s also the least used, alas.

Overall, I like and recommend Alien Frontiers. It’s just fresh enough to be fun, and it does feel like you have enough control over what you’re doing. I do wonder if it would hold up to heavy play, though. It’s not quick enough to be filler, and I don’t yet see much variation in the narrative from game to game. Learning curve will keep things fresh for a handful of plays, but let’s see if it lives past that.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
I'm normally not that much of a fan of dice placement games, but this one has a lot of ways to manipulate your dice. This manipulation doesn't make me like the game more because of mitigating luck per se, but instead my normal problem with dice placement is rolling before vs. after removes the tension of the risk-reward of your action. The manipulation doesn't fix that, but adds a lot of tactics on how you can manipulate your dice. I wish however there was more long term planning; since there are so many spots that basically do the same thing in different ways and the spots available to me can be completely different from turn to turn, it feels very much like I'm just playing for the here and now. That, the lack of buildings, and no Intrigue cards are probably why I still like Lords of Waterdeep more, despite the cards/tokens that give you powers and the tactics of the dice manipulation. It is kind of cool there is an area control aspect for the abilities, but that along with the fact you can get extra dice in this game means there is runaway leader, or there would be if there wasn't a very blatant direct attack. I wish the attack was more subtle in its approach. Like Lords of Waterdeep theme is mostly pasted on.

RATING: 7.5
Best played with full complement of 4. Possible to find yourself on the wrong end of a beating through no fault of your own if the dice fall badly, but quick enough to justify luck elements and it's a fairly light and enjoyable game in most respects. There is a reasonable amount of interaction through raiding and tech card effects, which helps to bring it to life. It is a little sparse in terms of strategic decisions though, since the way the dice fall imposes luck-based constraints on your play, so it is important to not go into it expecting something it's not. It also means that for the game to be enjoyable you need next to zero downtime and I would recommend playing with a pretty strict timer or a conscious effort by all players to allocate dice as soon as humanly possible.

RATING: 7
The pulp Sci-Fi theme sets this game apart from many other games with similar mechanics, and I really get a kick out of it. The components are of a good quality, although the Chinese-printed board is a bit prone to warping.

This is worker placement with dice, a similar idea to Kingsburg, but with much better execution. There's a good amount of player interaction, both direct (stealing cards, competing for territory) and indirect (blocking spaces).

Gameplay is a little bit slow as a 4 player game, with significant downtime (it's not really possible to plan ahead), but as a 2 player game it's quite satisfying.

RATING: 3
Excuse me, but, how did this one get into the top 100 on BGG?

This is a worker placement game that is driven by dice rolls. On top of that, there are some overpowered cards. Ok, you can steal them away, but I really don't like games where you need to aggressively steal away another players results of hard work. And then, the theme is, sci-fi. I don't like sci-fi games, and in fact, the material looks sci-fi (except the dice, euh ships), but it really feels like a dice rolling game. And the game has this "leader runaway" problem, where, once you are winning, there is no way to catch you up since you have so much more advantages, that it's almost impossible. 

Definitely not a game for me. 

Minus: 1 (not with 2) - 3 (dice.. pff) - 5 - 7 - 8 (runaway leader) - 9 - [-1]

RATING: 7
4 / 5 - Very Good

All the ingredients are right for a good Euro-style game and this is certainly the best of the Euro-dice crowd. In addition to cunningly solving the manner in which random and strategy can pull in different directions by simply ensuring that your rolls always leave you in a position to do something useful, it also has a surprisingly and delightfully mean streak of player interaction. But I have a nasty suspicion that it engineers close finishes that are too close - detracting from the early parts of the game which feel kind of pointless, and resulting in far too many drawn games. See my review for more details: http://fortressat.com/index.php/reviews-toc/2614-alien-frontiers-review

RATING: 7
An interesting and entertaining dice game which is a combination of worker placement, take-that actions, area control and resource management. Simple mechanisms and well thought out costings for actions make this a very good game at its shorter length - the long game, however, outstays its welcome. I give the short game an 8, but the long would be at least a couple of points lower.

This is a decent and entertaining dice game with the caveat that it's not Troyes. By which I mean that Troyes is, so far, the best and most innovative dice game I've played. Alien Frontiers is a very good game, but it's not Troyes.

RATING: 10

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A worker placement in space. The Players have to colonize a new planet. Dice represent ships and depending on what you roll you can use orbital stations which provide you with different benefits: give you ore or fuel (the two currencies), allow you to build more ships, colonize the planet and more. The colonized areas of the planet increase benefits from orbital facilities and (most importantly) give VPs.

It is a very good game and it is easy to pick up by new players. There are usually enough choices that you can't be screwed by bad rolls.

I recommend playing with Factions after getting a hang of the basic game. They add additional facilities and also give players special abilities as well as hidden objectives.

I would recommend playing wit 2 or 3 players. A four players game can drag a bit and be a bit random, because it doesn't allow for a lot of planning as the board will be completely different when your turn comes.

I would lie to try it out with one of the solo variants.

RATING: 6
I think this game is interesting but has some flaws.  It's a real hybrid between being a euro and a take-that luck infused Amerithrash.  In the end, it's too deterministic AFTER the dice are rolled, which is too lucky because there isn't enough roll mitigation built in.  The amount of take-that also feels out of whack.

The game is also setup to fundamentally encourage King-making.  This is probably group dependent, but it's a real potential pitfall built into the design.  There are also serious issues with AP.  I've seen non-AP players bog down.

I really have struggled to enjoy this game in comparison to the love showered on it.  What really kills it is the existence of many other, better, "dice worker placement" games.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Best w/3-4, 2-4:
Including Factions, Faction Pack 1, and Expansion Pack 1, and Terran Exchange Faction

I like this game very much. It is well balanced, it plays smoothly, it plays well with all player counts (but 3-4 is best) and I like the art style and theme.  Components are also top notch.  Gameplay is dice rolling / action selection / area control.  Players roll dice and try to assemble Poker style hands (two of a kind, three of a kind, straight, etc) which determine eligibility of action spaces.  Extra dice can be purchased, along with power up cards.  The ultimate goal is to build colonies on the planet Maxwell, which scores you points.  Learning the board layout is the major hurdle for new players as there are 9 different actions available (not to mention another 9 different planet locations with buffs), but the iconography on the board is well done, and somewhat intuitive.  After two or three rounds, new players should be up to speed on mechanics at least.  If I had one complaint about this game, it would be that it lacks tension.  You almost always have someplace to play your ships (dice) since the game is well balanced, so in a four player session the game seems to drag out a little even with normal speed players.  I can see why the game was designed for 4 player max.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
I quite enjoyed this game but didn't fall in love with it like I thought (wanted) to. I suspect the burden of expectation was at play here.

This dice allocation game is similar to others in that locations have requirements that must be rolled and then benefits are bestowed. But only 1 person can occupy the majority of locations at one time (like Kingsburg). 

The difference to Kingsburg though is that a player's dice will remain in place until a player's next turn, thus locking that position out to others. So timing is uber-important here and that leads to the need to plan a turn or two in advance.

Driving the game is the goal of collecting the most VP's and this is largely done by controlling sections of a moon. Thus area-control mechanics are in play in Alien Frontiers and several locations pertain to ships, fuelling them and getting them to the moon.

This very much reminded me of Mission Red Planet even if the mechanics vary greatly.

I'll probably end up getting it at some point purely because I am a dice allocation fan. But at present it sits on a par with Alea Iacta Est and Stone Age for me.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
Alien Frontiers looks terrific and after a proper reading of the rules, one will probably find the game quite elegant and teachable. The reality of it is unfortunately frustrating on multiple counts.

Dice allocation is an interesting mechanic, but comes with the potential pitfall of your rolls determining an obvious course of action. In order to prevent that, the game refrains from granting every die value an optimal use (while strangely still mostly rewarding 6s higher than 1s). Instead, it tries to have you get a mix of high rolls, straight suits and pairs/three-of-a-kind... which do little for avoiding a pecking order of best moves. For instance lunar mineral being harder to harvest than solar energy, the raiding action is absolutely fantastic, because it seems to have been balanced for stealing an average of both resources. But in practice, stealing 4 minerals is not only the most efficient way to acquire minerals in the game, but also damages your opponents in an equal measure.

That's not even the biggest offender. Whenever you roll as pair while your bank contains the resources necessary to build a new die, you just always do, because more dice snowball into more actions per turn that accelerate getting even more dice. And while bash-that-leader-with-6-dice is a thing, on the opposite end of the spectrum someone behind in dice, especially if the gap formed early, can mostly kiss goodbye to their chances at winning. And losing a 90 minute game because of randomness in the first 10 is rather annoying.

Oh wait, did I say "90 minute"? That's the official duration printed on the box, but there's a catch: since the basic mechanics are bit too dependent on luck, technology cards have been implemented to allow you to do a wide variety of things. Notably dice manipulation. And between those effects, the one time trashing effect of the cards, the powers from regions controlled and the possible acquisition of new techs during your turn, calculating anything close to efficiency takes forever. Of course analysis paralysis is a player-driven problem; but here the game wants that problem to arise. Because as free die slots shift every turn and as you only reroll your dice at the beginning of your turn, you can't. Plan. Anything. Ahead. Of. Your. Turn. Which means that not only did I once witness a 15 minute turn, but I also had absolutely nothing to do during it. And when finally came my occasion to speed things up, I looked at my tech cards and my dice roll and spent a comparatively short time thinking, but which was still 100% downtime for everyone else.

RATING: 4
Space themed game aimed at establishing the most colonies to trigger game end, in which player with most victory points wins. Dice are rolled, then game proceeds as a worker placement game, but you need the correct numbers/ combinations to trigger certain effects. Two in game resources, solar energy (orange dscs) and ore (? grey cubes) need to be carefully managed. 

PROS
-Science fiction authors implemented in the names of different locations (e.g. Asimov crater).
-Technology cards attempt to minimize luck in the game. 
-Great board design and artwork. 

CONS
-Game is like Yahtzee.
-Difficult to plan ahead, meaning lots of downtime (roll then analysis paralysis).
-Usually just doing what the dice tell you. 
-First person to get extra dice usually wins, which is how I won my first game (based on luck, rolling doubles).
-Technology cards often seem convoluted and some are much better than others.
-Some technology cards prolong the game by excessive account keeping and continual stealing/ inhibiting others.
-Hard to manage resources, you may have the necessary resources to establish a colony for many turns but cant because you cannot trigger the required dice roll or the spot is always taken by those who can reproducibly. Then someone steals your resources (followed by you rolling a now useless dice combo letting you establish a colony!).

RATING: 3
Alien Frontiers is plain-old dull. By forcing each player to place all their Ships every one of their turns, AF removes all tension and the appearance of player interaction. Paying attention to other player's turns is boring and strategically useless. A player only needs to assess the board when their turn arrives.

AF has a runaway leader problem. Although the issue is potentially mitigated by the randomness of dice rolls, if a player rolls at least one 6 and a pair of any number consistently there is no way for others to keep pace. Basically, whoever is last to the Shipyard location will lose the game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A strategic, area-control dice game.  Come up with different sets of rolls to be able to use different action areas on the board that will allow you to do different things during the course of the game.  One action gives you resources, another action gives you more dice to roll, and another one allows you to place your pawns onto the planet everyone is trying to control.  Like most respected dice games, no matter what you roll you should be able to contend for the win with smart play.

One of my bigger qualms with this game is that when someone takes the lead in an area that someone else was in the lead in, the new leader gets +1, and the person that just lost control gets -1.  A 2 point swing would not be such a big deal, however the game is played to 4 points, which is huge.  

The game is fun and enjoyable, if a little on the lighter side, and I would certainly play it again.

RATING: 6.5
Alien Frontiers is a decent, lightweight dice-roller. It’s pretty quick and the luck of the dice shouldn’t put people off, as rolling high isn’t necessarily good (though not rolling any doubles throughout the game could be a problem). There is a fair bit of tit for tat in the game and plenty of opportunity to have a pop at your fellow players - all good, however, I felt the gap between winning and losing was rather too slender, so that the mixture of area majority and ‘take that’ meant the outcome could be a little arbitrary. You only get to place a maximum of six colonies throughout the game, so the territories don’t change hands very often. When they do it’s a two point swing. With an average winning score of 8, deciding who to knobble can quickly lead to bash the leader (I’m OK with that) or king making (not so much). Still, I’ve only played it once and my worries may not be as prevalent as I imagine.

RATING: 8.5
OK game.  Uses dice to control worker placement (doubles, triples, run of 3, minimum values, and higher values get more resources). Lots of "take that" player interaction in occupying work sites so others can't use them.  A bit of area control on a planet.  Controlling the area grants the owner a bonus when using the adjacent work site.  Resources are the dice (3 ships to start, 6 max), ore, fuel, cards (gain benefits when use), and colonies.  When a player plays his last colony the game ends and the winner is the one with the most points (# of colonies + # of controlled areas + any card bonuses (2)).

After many plays I like this one a bit more (bumped from 7.5 to 8).  Plays better with more but even with 2 is good.    Does have a bit of a run away leader problem.  Some combinations of cards/planet zone powers are "too" powerful and take combined action from other players to stop.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
[b]Alien Frontiers[/b]

[COLOR=#FF0000]A year later... thoughts and rating (6):[/COLOR]

[i]I really didn't enjoy my first play, especially since my kids really weren't having fun. A buddy of mine really wanted to play so I tried it again and enjoyed it a bit more. The design itself feels dated and really doesn't do anything that makes you want to keep bringing it to the table. I love the area control aspect of the game, but I don't enjoy the resource management since the decision space is so limited. This wouldn't be a deal breaker if the game was quick, but it can go long and begin to feel quite repetitive. Definitely not a game that I need to play again.[/i]

[COLOR=#FF0000]Initial thoughts and rating (6):[/COLOR]

[i]I have been wanting to play this for years and my buddy Mat let me borrow it to play with my kids. I really thought they would love it and I thought I would like it a lot more than Kingsburg.

After playing for over an hour I could tell that my kids were getting bored (mind you long games are not an issue with my kids, they'll sit and play Agricola with me). In the end nothing about the game grabbed them and they felt like they were repeating the same actions over and over and I kind of agree. There really isn't much variety in the actions you can take. Also waiting for each person to place all their dice gets old fast, especially by the time my daughter had 7 dice. I much prefer Kingsburg because there are a lot more choices you can make when placing your dice (a total of 16 advisers versus maybe 8 - 10 spaces in AF).

I would play it again if someone requested it, but it's not a game I would consider adding to our collection (I am grateful that I got a chance to try it out before buying it).[/i]

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
心得:
算是競速的遊戲
但是相對於建設可能比較強調是互動
遊戲過程如果沒有互動略顯平淡
相對看重骰運
選擇上算是不多(資源就兩種)
主要策略性應該是加上卡牌之後使用卡牌的方式
規則:
*起始一人一張科技卡，起始三顆骰放在維持碼頭(另外三顆放圖版外)，殖民token依遊戲人數拿取(2p:8 3p:7 4p:6) 骰數最大當起始玩家，順位補償(無-1燃料-1礦-1燃料+1礦)，長遊戲模式則都拿8個殖民token
*牌庫開三張科技卡，並保留棄牌區，依人數蓋掉不用的行動格
*每輪行動把先骰碼頭內的所有自己的骰，在依任意順序決定用骰跟科技卡
*放殖民token時得一分，如果是該區塊最多的在額外一分，跟拿該區域特權卡，得額外能力，如果失去該卡同時也失去一分，遊戲在有玩家把全部殖民token放完結束
*每人手上全部資源總數上限8個
*科技卡動作格:每次放骰都可以選擇棄三張重抽，只要放的骰和超過八就可拿一張要拿兩張的話就要兩組超過八的，不能拿重複的
*殖民推進區:放任意骰顆推進一個殖民地，推到第七格就可花費降落在星球區塊上，如果是最後一個殖民地在推但是骰到可以直接降落的骰點，可直接跳過去
*鐵礦區:放骰要大於等於目前在上面的
*交易區:放兩顆一樣的骰，骰數值是能量或鐵礦的匯率跟交換的次數
*雷達基地區:放順，如果手頭上的順比上面的大，可以把上面的踢走，此動作可選偷竊任一組合的四個能量及礦石(可不同玩家)或是一張卡片
*船廠:兩顆同樣骰，根據要建第幾艘船要付不同資源
*變化區:有四顆以上骰才可使用，直接降殖民地，下回合此骰回供應區
*科技卡:拿到就可用，花能量的每回合用一次，用過的不可在使用棄掉功能，一回合只能棄一張牌，牌用完把棄牌重洗在繼續

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Worker placement with dice (or sets of dice) used to claim action spaces. It's an ongoing worker placement - there's no end of round where you take your workers back, you just pick them up at the start of your turn, roll them, and then those spaces are blocked until your next turn.

Sometimes a low roll is good, sometimes a high roll, or a set or a run etc., but you have no control over what you roll so you're largely at the mercy of the dice, and then it's a case of puzzling out what your best move is., often there's only 1 or 2 good ways of using your dice.

I also felt that there was not enough asymmetry between the players positions - everything was about the same value to everyone. The game would have benefited from more/stronger special powers.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
A jazzed up To Court The King with some resource gathering stuff to get you into the area majorities race to earn the VPs needed to win, with enough "take that" to keep things level throughout until someone sneaks over the line. It has the same downside though, pure downtime, but here's it's magnified because the game takes 3 times longer. Your own turn is interesting though and that can save the game. Roll your dice. Allocate them to boxes on the board worker-placement style (blocking spaces from other players until you retrieve the dice at start of next turn) earn resources, steal them, or pay those resources in various ways to either get more dice or to parachute meeples onto the board into the area majority areas. Or go for tech cards which will allow you to manipulate dice rolls, acquire resources, etc, in all sorts of different ways. If you've acquired smartly, by mid-game your turns should be a pretty interesting turn of dice manipulation options and hopefully you've developed a means of getting meeples onto the board more efficiently than the other players. If you can do it under the radar so that you're stolen from just as much as the others, all the better. Towards the end, you can also discard those tech cards to move meeples between areas, which can be devastating to your score if you're a perceived or actual leader and you're the one hit. So the game all levels up, and hopefully your engine is still intact enough (after card stealing as well) to get you the win, getting your last meeple out there faster than anyone else at a point when you're in a position to manipulate the meeples / score to get you over the line scorewise. It's decent enough on your turns, but pretty boring watching analysis paralyis develop on other players' turns as their decision trees broaden. No story, no tension arc, no drama, this is just a long dice game. I'd like to think you can do clever plans towards the end to get you the win in a rush, but with all the take-that (which gets more prevalent as the game develops and people have more dice to spare, particularly in a 4 player, less with less), you can't take any planning too seriously. Having said that, I'm pretty sure this game doesn't want to be taken too seriously anyway and I'm generally happy to play it because I know my own turns will be interesting.

RATING: 9.5
Played the game twice last night with 3 other friends. We were addicted quickly. It's one of the best game mechanics we've played.

The game is phenomenal fun. Some interesting (and positive) aspects of our games were:

* The fact you rely on the luck of the dice, makes it very difficult to hold grudges against other players.
* The final rounds of a game are tons of fun because everyone is calculating each and every move to ensure maximum VP upon game end.
* The 'tie' rules were a great help in aiding us to determine how many points we receive for our tournament system.
* The game contains top notch components and is a solid design with more than enough plastic bags for storage.

Props to the designers, artists, play-testers, and manufacturers of the game.

This game has received the highest rating I've given a game on BoardGameGeek!

RATING: 8
A nice sci-fi game on building your economy by acquiring ships, constructing colonies, and competing for region control.  The use of dice as ships is a new mechanism (for me, anyway) that works pretty damned well, with different available to the players with different rolls.  Technology cards provide you ways to modify die rolls, affect the map, and otherwise influence play.

The one potentially valid shortcoming this game has is that the use of dice does mean that someone is likely to encounter the "If I'd only rolled a total that was 1 higher."  Or "If I'd only rolled another 6."  This is a potential problem, but not a large one since every player is likely to encounter it at some time throughout the game.  If these types of issues bother you, focus on acquiring technology that allows you to manipulate your ships (dice).  Or find something else. :)

Nice, light, perhaps a bit long for some.  But easy to teach and play and fun with excellent production values.

RATING: 8
I'm usually not a fan of the "roll dice and assign them" sub-genre, but this one really stood out as a winner.  I really liked how the dice-rolling wasn't all done at once--each player rolls his dice on his turn, and the spots he takes with those dice stay "blocked" until his next turn.  The flow of the game worked well too--you can get more dice to be able to do more things on your turn, but often to secure points you needed to give up those dice.  The various options on the board were varied and fun, and provided a good bit of interaction between players.

RATING: 6
A major problem with this game is that each player rolls his dice at the start of his turn, so any forward planning is impossible. Hence, the game has a ton of downtime during which players are doing nothing and cannot plan their next turns...

That said, I like the game well enough. There is opportunity for clever play. The length is about right for a game of this weight. 

There is one potential fix I heard proposed: Each player gets a 2nd set of dice in his color and a player screen. At the end of a player's turn, he rolls the dice behind the screen. While it would require a few tweaks, this would mean that players could largely plan turns in advance. I might even bump up the rating on this if such a system worked.

RATING: 3
Horrible game for 4 players. Lengthy downtime, and no preplanning is possible as you can't roll until your turn.

The only way to reliably have less reliance on luck is to gain extra ships (which takes luck) or to buy the artifacts that allow for some mitigation. (which takes luck) The players with extra ships will get more actions, and have better odds of ideal roles. The fourth player is liable to be shut out of getting a new ship for multiple rounds. 

The game has a problem with runaway leaders, forcing players to pile on the leader or king-make.

Any combination of bad rolls and blocking players will ensure you can do nothing useful on your actions.

The Pirate Raid is game-breakingly overpowered. If your dice allow it and you can steal one or more ore, always take this action.

The "balancing" mechanic for going last in a four-player game is absolute rubbish. (Be the only player to take your first turn with all of the best spaces claimed, to get a pittance of fuel that helps with next to nothing. Seriously? I would recommend that you give the 2nd player one fuel, the 3rd player one ore, and the 4th player starts with one of each, except that it would just be that much MORE of a swing to the first player that rolls a straight and raids on his first turn.)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Simple, fast-paced (particularly if played with 2 or 3 players) game, that is a mix of worker placement and area control with cool components. The workers (thematically called "ships") are actually die, that are rolled each turn and used to activate different spaces in the board. Rolls can be modified via cards but nearly all rolls are of some use.

Gameplay feel changes as the game evolves. In the early game, with few dice and few or no cards to change the rolls, you have to make the most out of limited choices. By the endgame players have more dice and roll-changing cards to boot, so you can turn almost any roll into anything you want - and it becomes much more an exercise of finding the most efficient allocation for the dice among several promising options.

I'm curious to see how factions with special powers alter the game.

RATING: 6
This game suffers from some glaring design flaws that prevent it from being the great medium-length filler I'd hoped it would be.

Inability to plan for your next turn makes AP and downtime horrendous. It's not so bad in the early turns where players have few dice and Alien Tech. But as those increase, so does the downtime.

The game is simply too long for its weight. Our 4p game took roughly 2.5 hours plus explanation.

There is too much bash-the-leader such that the winner can feel they got there by being lucky or not being picked on as much as the other players were.

Early ships are too important, meaning there is really only one correct strategy--gather more ships early, establish colonies late.

What prevents this game from being a 4 or even a 3 is that it has a cool theme and very nice graphic design. It is a pleasure to look at and interact with the components, even if the game itself is mediocre.

RATING: 7
It has the dice-as-workers of Kingsburg combined with the theme, special powers and chunky scoring of Cosmic Encounter. I like it a lot more than Kingsburg because there seem to be more ways of using your dice, and low rolls don't seem to be a problem at all (oh, and there are no effing Orcs). I enjoyed how you could put creative strings of actions together involving the colony powers and alien techs, and how you often seemed to be 'just one short' of the perfect plan. This could allow AP to set in once players have lots of dice, but this really isn't the sort of game you should be agonising over. After one play, Alien Frontiers seems to be the great middleweight dice-based worker placement game that Stone Age should have been.

RATING: 7
I probably rated this a point below what I should have, but I didn't want to enter a .5 rating so I made a choice. Oh well.

Alien Frontiers makes worker placement with "variable" workers actually function in an entertaining way. Unlike a game like Kingsburg, where higher rolls are genrally better, you can still make relevant plays with low rolls.

Also, the components in the game are great. At least in the 4th edition, there are tuckboxes for individual player components and the other game materials. This is great, and more game manufacturers should implement this if they're too pressed to make custom inserts for games.

RATING: 8
Pros and Cons as a 2-Player Game

Pros:
- Just plain fun. I love rolling dice.
- Fairly quick setup.
- Your options are controlled by the roll of the dice, but we almost never felt stuck.
- Beautiful game with a fun theme.
- Lots of "take that" options without feeling like you're getting picked on.

Cons:
- While still fun for 2, it lacks a lot of the struggle to control regions on the planet. See below for some (untested) ideas to improve this.
- The "Factions" may not work well for 2. There's already too little competition for docking stations; wouldn't want to add even more.

Unsure:
- Kind of a money sink. My completionism required I buy the upgrade pack, Factions, Factions#1, AND the kickstarter rocket dice AND the 4th printing.

== 2013-03-03 - R:8.9

I wanted to play this a few more times before giving it a rating, but given how much time I spend just thinking about it, I think I'm going to go ahead and prematurely admit I love it. This is what I wanted 'To Court the King' to feel like. Played once with my wife and once with my brother.

== 2013-04-07 - R:8.9

Have now played about six times, all 2 player. There's something missing in a 2p game; there's nowhere near enough competition for regions on the planet and some of the agenda cards are hard to satisfy with only 2. I've been thinking up some [article=12269837]variants[/article] to help.

== 2013-04-20 - R:8.9

One of the more common criticisms of dice games where you can increase the number of dice you can roll is that the first person to luck out and get an extra die has a pretty big advantage. This is an even larger issue in Alien Frontiers because when you get your extra ships, you also hinder the ability of others to immediately catch up. Here's a variant I haven't tried. "Everyone starts with six ships". This would also make for a pretty quick game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 1
Pros:
1. Dice placement is always fun (or so I thought)
2. Decent amount of player interaction

Cons:
1. Take that is very strong in this game and allows you to pick on one player
2. The best spot in the game is the outpost that allows you to steal 4 resources from another player. It can take upwards of 5 turns to gain 4 resources, but they can be taken away in 1 turn.
3. No difficult decisions. There are very few decisions to be made in this game making what to do with the dice very easy.

Summary: Honestly, the alien outpost breaks this game. Anytime you can play that spot, you have to play that spot. That spot has 3 positives, you gain 4 resources (the most efficient way to gain resources), you protect yourself from that spot, and you severely injure another player by taking all his resources. The player who is able to play that spot the most will win this game the very large majority of the time. This classifies the game as broken to me. The people who love this game are the people who haven't done the math to see how badly the outpost breaks the game. Even ignoring the outpost, there are no tough decisions in this game. The majority of the time what you need to do based on your dice roll is very clear meaning this game would still be below a 5 even without the broken mechanic. There's so many better dice placement games to play, so just skip this one.

A good game of Frontiers is very entertaining- so learn the right time to make your move for the win!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.9
The most analytical dice game I've ever played.  Just an excellent way to utilize dice without the dice roll completely screwing you.  Each turn plays almost like a puzzle.  Though there are some strategic directions overall it appears how you tactically use you dice that is most important. Just a great thinking game, however, this game can be slowed by analysis paralysis thus extensive downtime is not unusual.  With two players it's not bad I imagine 4 players it can be really bad.  This is because there is not much to think about while others are playing because you need to know you dice roll and you don't know what spots will be taken before the start of you your turn.  Very unique but not as fun as the hype  for me but a very good game.  Update:  played with Factions and this changes the game a little from a pure tactile same-y type game and adds more a strategic element as you try to optimize your factions powers.  Game is an 8.3 with Factions which is really the only way I'd play from now on.  It give the game some tactical/strategical balance.

RATING: 6
Games with dice are having a bit of a comeback, which is good because I grew up with games that used dice and lots of them were very good games. The best ones, games like Kingsburg, have taken on board Euro sensibilities and use the dice to limit a players options while not having any one dice roll strictly better or worse than another. 
Alien Frontiers doesn't do any of that.When you roll the dice it gives a huge range of possible actions, because the dice can be applied in a variety of orders and can usually be manipulated in a variety of ways. And generally some dice rolls are just strictly better than others. 
This doesn't make Alien Frontiers a bad game, you have a lot of fun trying to get your six colonies placed (this is very much Cosmic Encounters meets Kingsburg) and building up your stock of special powers and resources. However, the game still hinges a lot on the dice, roll well and win. And the multitude of choices immediately after the roll means it is a bad game if a player suffers from analysis paralysis.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
At its core, this is a very good game, but it's marred by three main problems:

1. It's too easy to hog the two most powerful spaces: stealing and terraforming.  With the right alien artifacts, you can hold a monopoly on these spaces, and easily dominate the game.  

2. The game suffers from a version of middle child syndrome, where it's not quite a casual game and not quite a solid strategic one either.  It's too long to be casual, and it's too random (both from dice rolls and opponents randomly stealing what you have) to be strategic.

3. There's too much time between turns.  Since you won't know what you have to work with until it's your turn and you roll, there's nothing for you to do but sit and wait for your turn, and if your opponent's the kind to take his time, it can get really boring.

All that said, there is a lot of great aspects to the game.  The gameplay is streamlined, balanced, and a lot of fun.  I just wish it was either a bit deeper, or a bit quicker.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This game has plenty to like about it, and indeed I really enjoyed it when we first got it. But my enthusiasm for it has faded so much that, while I still respect it as a good design, I almost never want to play it any more.

First, the good points: the basic worker-placement-with-dice mechanics, where each action spot requires particular combinations of dice (pairs, sequences, higher values than previously placed, and so on), along with the ability to manipulate your dice by use of the Alien Tech cards, are still very fresh-feeling, and allow for a good mix of randomness and strategy. It always feels satisfying to turn an unpromising-looking roll into a good turn by finding a good "combo" (involving cards and the right sequence of actions to take). The fact that each of the territories which you can control on the planet gives you a particular special ability - which can be taken away by other players depriving you of control - adds another welcome aspect to the game.

But for me personally, the randomness is a bit too much. In the early game - as in many worker-placement games - the biggest priority is getting an extra worker (here you do that at the "shipyard" spot, as workers represent spaceships). This requires you to roll a pair - but, even in conjunction with the dice-manipulation abilities on the cards, it can happen that one player is unable to do this for several turns in a row at the start of the game (of course, it's much harder when you start with only 3 dice), which tends to put them more or less out of the game. And in the late game, it's even worse than this, because the key spot then is the "terraforming station" (place a 6 to place a new colony - the things which basically you are racing to put on the planet in order to win the game - at the cost of losing that die, but that hardly matters if it's your last or second-to-last colony). The die placed there still locks everyone else out of that spot, as it doesn't actually leave the board until the start of your next turn - so if you can roll a 6 (or create one from your roll and tech card abilities) every turn at the end of the game, you can basically lock down a win.

In addition to this, the game is extremely AP-prone, because of the sheer number of different possibilities you have when you have 5 or 6 dice and 3 or 4 alien tech cards, not to mention that the order in which you place your dice to get stuff can be very important (you can place a couple first to get a new tech card and then instantly use it, for example). In combination with the fact that you only roll your dice at the start of your turn, thus not allowing you to really plan your turn in advance, this tends to reduce things to a snail's pace as the game goes on. And this is a particular flaw in a game which is clearly intended to be a very interactive "social" type game. (There are plenty of "take that" aspects, which while not especially vicious, can create plenty of table talk.)

Overall, I am really in two minds about this game. I really do admire it in many ways, but ultimately you have to rate a game based on how much fun it is to play, and, while I don't hate AF by any means, most of the time it isn't particularly fun for me and I'd never actively choose to play it any more. Hence I am reducing my rating - previously it was 7, but compared to a lot of the other games which I give that rating to, I think I have to make this one a 6.

RATING: 7.5
Roll your dice (ships), then decide how to best utilize them in placement to accumulate resources and try to colonize the  planet.  But those dice remain on the board so you also have the opportunity to block your opponents.  There is definitely a "take that"/screwage factor in this game.  Besides blocking, there are cards that let you steal your opponents resources, do things such as develop or utilize the cards at a lower cost, and even cards to blast your opponents dice (ships) out of an area or block them from stealing. 

Planning what to do on your turn as play moves around the table is key - but be prepared to adapt as your options might not be available.  The right tactics are key.

RATING: 9
OK, so you've introduced your group, a bunch of designer game virgins to Catan. They love it. They're asking to play it over and over again. 
You know you need something new to pull them out of Catan before that's all they'll play. What do you get before your entire group turns into Klaus Teuber's howling swine?
Alien Frontiers, of course! This game plays slightly longer than Catan, with many of the same mechanics, plus with a cool retro spacey vibe.
It has dice, plus you can build more dice! It has areas to colonize and cool little snow-globe-esque future cities! And it even has (sort of) the robber mechanic from Catan. And it does all this without the equivalent of the trading system that makes Brenda from Mississauga ask if anyone wants her sheep every turn!
This might be the perfect game to wean your group off of Catan.
I played this with a total of three players, myself included. It played perfectly with that number, and I'm actually a little bit worried as to how it plays with 4, as the strategy of blocking off important parts of the board with your own dice gets invalidated a bit (then again, that strategy totally failed me; I finished in a close third place.)

RATING: 8
Had been wanting to try this one for a while, but it always seemed to get bumped to the back burner by something else.  I also have kind of an aversion to area-control, mostly due to being traumatized by El Grande.  Fortunately, this combines something I love - dice manipulation with area control to make an interesting game experience.  The theme seems a little pasted-on, but the gameplay is fun and once you know what you're doing, the downtime is minimal.  I can see why this is so highly rated on here.  I also like how the board gets artificially tightened with less than 4 players.  I love tightness.  I don't know if I would like playing it with two, tho.  It's a good game, that isn't overly long and plays quickly.  Despite this, my only knocks on it are that it seems like it goes one round longer than it should and there can be a bit of a kingmaker element to it.  A solid 8.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.75
Update: I have had a chance to play this a little bit more and I have a slightly different outlook. I will be honest, I really enjoy dice euros. This game is good in my opinion but suffers from a few things that should be considered. First off, acquiring additional dice while playing the game can make or break your overall playing experience. Another factor that irritated some players that I have played with is the ability to steal resources and cards. This by itself can frustrate players to never play again. Finally, the leader bashing and king making aspect leaves me disappointed. On a positive note, the sci-fi setting as well as the layout/design are very good.

The problem is that this game has various minor irritants that affect certain types of players. When another player is not enjoying themselves and just wishes the game was over with it ruins the experience for me.

In closing, players prone to AP will have significant issues with this game. Players irritated by having resources and cards stolen from them will have issues. As I mentioned, I really enjoy the game as I am fond of dice euros. The problem is that I have yet to find anyone else in my various gaming groups that is really enthusiastic about this one, which is a shame.

RATING: 7.75
#76 on my Top 100 Games of All Time (2015)

Using dice as workers is something I like and this game does that really well. Players deploy their dice to various sectors on the board to acquire resources/ability cards and colonize. Each sector on the board has its own requirement on what you need to roll to place there. There is quite a bit of player interaction as you fight over colonization and can block other players from certain areas.

Acquired: April 2014 - Kickstarter
Sold: March 2016 - BGG Auction
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Worker-placement game with 'Kingsburg' dice elements. Players are rolling and placing dice to get resources to build more ships (aka dice). Object is to place colonies on a central board. 1VP per colony placed and 1vp for bonus tile (can be taken by majority in space on board). Special tech cards add chaos and options to the mix.

Clips along nicely but players can get effectively hosed if they are subject to the 'take that' raiders element. Not fun to sit through a game you can't win. Balance feels 'off'. The fact that downstream players after the first get extra resources should give indication of balance issues.

Good solid game.

Szeretem a kockajátékokat. Kíváncsi voltam a Kingsubrg és az Alea Iacta Est után mit tud felmutatni az Alien Frontiers. A trónkövetelő sok mindent megtett, de nem változott a király személye. Nem sokon múlt a siker. Kiegyenlített játék, sci-fi téma, a kockák lehelyezésének, fennmaradásának mechanizmusai és az ikonozás nagyon jól sikerült. Az összképen a feliratos kártyák és azok kombinációs lehetőségének kisebb nehézsége csak nagyon kis mértékben ront számomra. Több játék után ez szerintem nem gond. Viszont a konfliktusforrás megjelenése rablásban az nem jött be. Sosem szeretem a rablás lehetőségét a játékokban. Ahogy az Endeavorból így ebből sem hiányzott volna egyáltalán, ha nem kerül bele. Ugyanúgy, mint az említett játékban ebben sem túl veszélyes ez a dolog, de azért több játékos esetén a királycsinálás lehetőségét adja meg. Kellemes, jó kockajáték az Alien Frontiers, amiben lehet kombinálni és jó szórakozást nyújt. Véleményem szerint ketten lehet a legjobb a játékélmény. Nem sokon múlt, de nálam maradt az Alea Iacta Est a kockajátékok királya. Körülbelül nekem egy szinten van a Kingsburggel, de nem hasonlíthatóak össze, teljesen más a két játék.

RATING: 7.5
Nice worker placement game where you manipulate dice to get the actions that you need to win the game.  It feels like someone played Mission: Red Planet and started this design by trying to figure out what would make M:RP fun; as a joke they said "dice" and then discovered they were right. 

There are a number of great things going on here.  I especially like that you get points for every guy down despite not having control, it keeps the game going forward.  But, I can't help but feel that you just kind of do the same stuff, until one player ends the game with more stuff.  Mid to late game can have more downtime as you get more actions and more abilities to manipulate your dice.

All in all it is a standard worker placement/engine euro. I wouldn't run out to get this, but I'd totally trade a game that I don't like for it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A dice-based worker-placement/action-selection, area-control game: roll dice, place in allowable areas to use those actions. Pretty good, really; but:
- that one action that allows you to steal 4 resources is VERY annoying; if 2 people have the holographic projector, the other can get hit continually by that power!
- [b]too much downtime[/b]; it would be nicer if you could roll your dice and plan what to do for next turn; of course, that wouldn't work here since some spaces will be occupied. Maybe it would work, if you had another set of dice and kept your roll hidden (you could at least make SOME kind of plan)?
- the game seems to be [b]too long[/b] for what's happening here; if it really finished in 60min, I'd probably like it a little more.

--- Summary ---
The board shows a planet with several regions on it and several orbital stations around it. The stations are the Actions in the game; they give you energy or ore, allow you to build more ships (use more dice), colonize areas of the planet, by alien tech cards, or steal a card OR resources (ore, energy) from other players. Each colony placed = 1 VP. Having control of a planetary region (more colonies than any other single player) gives you +1VP and a special power associated with that region -- almost all of which improve an associated action in some way (e.g., better trade ratio for energy to ore, pay less to colonize, etc.). 

On your turn, roll your available ships (start with 3d6) and allocate amongst the actions as you are able (each has different rules for allowable placements: e.g., pair, triplet, 3-die straight, at least as many pips as highest die already present, etc.). May use previously acquired tech cards (start with 1; may never have duplicates; all are face up and public) as you like, each card once/turn. At end of turn, discard down to 8 total resources.

Game ends when someone places their last colony onto planet; player with most VP wins.

RATING: N/A
A new dice mechanic that unfortunately doesn't work for me. I like the way the game is organized but I think you have to be lucky to play the game. In other words if you're lucky you'll win the game for sure. There's no catch-up mechanism meaning that the player that is ahead stays ahead. First part of the game makes you go for more dice. It's an obvious choice. You need a pair of dice to go for it. Two things can happen here: you have a pair of dice which is great and you nail it. You don't get a pair of dice. Period! There's no way to turn around things unless you can get a special card that gives you permission to modify your results. But that's another way of putting luck working for you - or against you. 
So, since I've rolled the dice about 5 times before getting my first pair, and so did another player. And since the third player got that pair of dice early (not just one but two!) I guess we could all leave the table after those first 3/4 turns. The game was finished by then. I know this now. Unfortunately I didn't know it back then!

Another unfavored point to the game is the lack of real area control. A few stations during the game is what players get and these are supposed to be used to control a few regions in the planet. It simply doesn't work. One because they are only a few specially at the beginning. Two because there are too many regions to have a meaningful dispute specially with three players only.

Alien Frontiers doesn't even have a bit of suspense to the victory because everything that matters is open information.

A few changes in how players could modify the rolls and how to balance gameplay by getting a catch-up mechanism would improve the game IMO.
(R)

RATING: 5
I've always felt that offering players too many optimization choices is not great game design, and when you give players the ability to change the pips on dice, you're inviting a level of analysis paralysis that is no longer so much about making tough, dramatic decisions as sorting through a large number of humdrum mathematical options to find the one that happens to work in that moment. Games like Alien Frontiers and Kingsburg suffer this.

RATING: 6
Full of dice but they work well here; there are several ways to manage your luck and a few sinks so even really awful rolls do [i]some[/i] good. There isn't a whole lot of strategy (no engine building, no combos) and it all seems so well-balanced that it doesn't much matter what you do -- and this closeness compounds the luck factor. Throw in the possibilities for AP and downtime and there's just not much there to love.

It's a decent enough light game but there are better dice games these days: this is one I would never choose over Burgundy or Troyes.

RATING: 7.5
[Clever Mojo Games 2010 edition] {Space; dice-rolling, worker-placement, area-control; scalable} Alien Frontier is a meaty game with dice.  Player races to build colonies at different territories.  It's fun and interactive.  A typical game runs around 60 minutes.  Yet, it is not as thematic as I expected.  Decisions are meaningful in Alien Frontier - players have to smartly place their 'fleet' to correct location to build up the engine, players have to decision which territory to control and which power to use, etc.  Competition of territory control and space ports is tense - it usually happens that you cannot get what you want because the docks are occupied by previous players.

The rulebook is very clear.  It is very simple to learn and teach.  The game components are as good as games from big publishers.

It is a very good game.  I am so glad that I own this game at the first print run.  And, I expect to see it being more popular in future when it is more accessible.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Very good space colonization game where the dice you use represent your ships. There are a number of options available to you, such as obtaining resources or technology, build new ships (gain extra dice thereby increasing your options), colonize the planet etc. Many of the options seem obvious at first as they all have conditions attached to them and the dice you assign to them have to meet those conditions. However, as the game progresses and you obtain more dice and more modifiers, the decisions you face and strategies you can adopt increase quite considerably. The gameplay also marries very well with the theme in 'Alien Frontiers', which can often be a failing in this type of game where the theme can sometimes feel like something of an afterthought.

RATING: 7
Good light euro-game. Has a very nice cadans in game flow. What it does best is allowing you to enjoy yourself in a very easy going fashion. No really harsh decisions, no nailbiting tension but certainly no boredom and downtime either. Really nice game to play which will attract a great diversity of people.

For hardcore gamers this game will be on the light side, though. 

Only drawback we had is the really high-powered raiding action coupled with the Decoy, which in a 3 player game almost shut out a third player with decidedly less ships, who got raided almost every turn by his (inexperienced) neighbour. But this might be start-up problems. 

Still we feel the raiding action is a bit too powerful. We will tone it down to three resources of choice raided and get rid of both Decoy cards in the tech deck. This hopefully improves the game even more.

But all in all, nice components, good gameflow, not too heavy and enjoyable all around. Good, solid game.

Due to lack of playtime, sold off spring 2015

RATING: 8
2015/4/25: Played it again a few years alter and this time it really clicked. The design holds up nicely and clearly surpasses the hype of a few years ago. The fact that you have lots of option and you never feel like you can't do anything with your dice really helps.

2011/6/10: Certainly one of the most polished games of the recent wave of "Dice-Games". OTOH, Alien Frontiers feels a bit overproduced and, for my taste, it takes a bit too long for a game of it's kind. All that said and given the hype this game has received on BGG, I feel kind of underwhelmed. Certainly a nice game and I'd play again anytime - but I don't need to own it.

RATING: 8
Alien Frontiers is a nice little dice placement game.  Unlike Kingsburg, which it is oft compared to, I feel that rolling low numbers will not hurt you here, which levels out the game. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Alien Frontiers can be an enjoyable mid-weight worker placement game that uses dice instead of static pieces. Each turn you roll your "ships" (dice) and then place them at various stations around the board. The goal is to gather resources and build colonies on the mysterious planet in the center of the board. The game has beautiful graphic design and is well put together mechanically. The space theme is a bonus for me.

But all of these things cannot, unfortunately, overcome my two major criticisms.

First, the game encourages players to pick on the leader. Scoring is constantly updated to reflect your current status. So if someone attacks your territory, you lose points while the attacker gains points. The Raider's Outpost also allows others to steal your resources and Alien Tech (special ability) cards. While there are ways to mitigate the damage, there is almost always a way for the group to bring down the leader. The effect is amplified by the fact that, in most games, you are only fighting over 6-10 points total. The result of all this attacking is that your choices matter less. You cannot gain a meaningful lead, and so it is more important to ride the wave of group-think than to play well on your own merits. In a game of diplomacy or negotiation, I wouldn't mind the jockeying, but in a game that ostensibly emphasizes resource manipulation, the politics seems out of place.

Second, downtime is an issue. And downtime, for me, is the kiss of death. The player in Alien Frontiers is presented with a wealth of options each turn. For people who play slowly and want to work through each set of options before taking action, turns can drag on and on. In one recent game, I was able to play almost an entire game of Backgammon on the side while waiting for the turn to come back around to me. And what do the over-analyzing players gain from all this computation? Not much, unfortunately, because as soon as they gain a lead, a couple swift kicks later they are back in the pack.

As a two-player game, where actions are zero sum and downtime is minimized, or with a light-hearted group of fast-playing people, Alien Frontiers is still enjoyable. But as a game I want to play often, it simply doesn't measure up.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.05
The long downtime slightly hurts the game, but the many ways to do stuff means you won't be dependant on luck for long. Pair it with an awesome theme, a fun game with dice, and you have yourself a winner.

RATING: 6
KINGSBURG ... IN ... SPACE (dramatic flourish here)! Before I start listing some quibbles, let me say straight up that I enjoy Alien Frontiers. I get the kind of kick out of this that I felt palpably lacking in Ad Astra, despite the similar themes. Now, that said, this isn't perfect. As with Kingsburg, there are ways to mitigate bad rolls to some extent, but just as with Kingsburg, if you continually roll low numbers you're likely to struggle here. Alien Frontiers also punishes folks something fierce if they're unable to roll doubles (or triples) - yes, you can eventually buy alien tech cards that let you manipulate the dice to make doubles, but by then if you haven't rolled natural doubles to get more ships (dice) early, you're in a deep hole. Alien Frontiers also has enough opportunities for screwage that kingmaking is a possible issue that I'll be keeping an eye on. But again, quibbles. Alien Frontiers is a light dice-rolling romp that doesn't need to be perfect to be fun. Update: Kingmaking and end-game weirdness continue to be an issue here, bringing this down in my ratings. I still enjoy the mechanics, but the end game is all about who you're going to take points away from and who benefits from that. Disappointing to the point where I'm likely to trade this.
