=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 3
First Impression:
An area control dicey euro with a working retro sci-fi theme.

What I liked: 
a) The theme is fine, and the retro graphics, namings as well as the clever cards all create a fine setting for the game. 
b) The components are also fine, and I really liked the little colony tokens. 
c) Contrary to early and late parts of the game, the mid game is sufficiently strategic, as by then most players have enough dice + cards to at least have some choices and they're not yet forced to bash the leader.

What I didn't like: 
a) The whole area control aspect in medium strategy games falls completely flat for me. That's a personal preference, as when there is area control in a game, I want it to be bloody and with negotiation. Or with some hidden information. Otherwise there is just too much kingmaking and bash the leader for my liking. 
b) The "steal" action makes the bashing-the-leader aspect even worse. 
c) The implementation of the dice is just plain bad. The classic high rolls beat low rolls applies here as well (with the exception of the trader which is not enough to counter this), and in addition there are many lucky rolls all around. It doesn't feel tactical at all, and you can hardly ever pick where you want to go, your dice usually lead you - especially when you have just a few of them.
d) The start up of the game is also ridiculsly random, as there are cards you want at the start of the game, cards for the mid game, and cards for the late game. This combined with the dice randomness leads to a completely random first third of the game, where some players will take the lead just because they had a better start and rolled better, and some will fall behind. 
e) The last part of the game is even worse though, and it reminds me a lot of Cyclades. Since one has to place 6 colonies to end the game, but not necesserily win it, once a player has put down 5 colonies he's bashed in any possible way from everyone else because he MAY win. Thus, the rest soon catch up, and the game ends up with most players close to winning, all bashing the next one ready to, until one succeeds. The 2h you played before that, with all it's randomness and strategy matters for nothing at the end. If you were lucky and good, the others will simply beat you down until they catch up. 
f) Finally and most importantly, the game really suffers from downtime, as there is absolutely nothing you can do, or even plan at the turn of any other player, and the dice rolling, and card searching, and special abilities reading only makes this worse.

This game uses a couple of mechanisms I really dislike (dice rolling and area control in a euro game), and adds downtime, randomness, bashing-the-leader encouragment and a terrible endgame. I can't see myself playing this ever again on my own, but I'll try to if someone insists just to give it a second chance.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

I can certainly see how this game is not going to be for everyone and I can see where the idea of replay is slim as just a base game goes but I like it. I'd play from time to time and enjoy the crap out of it I'm sure. The theme is excellent and I love the Sci-Fi authors being implemented. All in all a pretty cool game.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
The goal of the game is to get the most point, which is done by sending your colony ships to the moon (or possibly some other planet... I didn't really figure that part out). You get 1 point for every colony you send, and 1 point for every territory you control. Only there's an area control contest going on because there aren't enough territories on the moon, and all of them come with cool abilities.

On your turn you roll you dice and then place them on the board. Various actions have placement restrictions of needing high numbers, sequences, pairs, low numbers etc. Additionally, you only take your dice back at the start of your next turn. In the interim they stay out and block other players. There are cards you can purchase in the game, which let you do cool dice manipulation things, and a few other special powers.

Most of the actions in the game revolve around getting energy and ore, the game's two currencies, and getting more dice, or ways to get card, or launching colony ships.

The game ends whenever any player gets his last colony launched... which generally, but not always, will mean that player wins.

The game was interesting. There's some strategy going on. It's not a game I'd want to play all the time, but it was fun.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Love Alien Frontiers, even though I never win because dice. The little colonies are my favourite board gaming component.

RATING: 3
In any game, I think it's infuriating to have to listen to other people talk through special power combos as they execute them: experience suggests to me that people (including me) cannot help but sound smug doing so. Something like Dominion can succeed in spite of this because you only ever have to be half-listening to your opponent: you can dedicate a good portion of your attention to planning your own turn instead. In Alien Frontiers, you can't really do so: because you don't know what your dice will let you do until the start of your turn, there's no meaningful planning to hide behind, and therefore you'll find yourself with nothing to do but listen to each and every bit of your opponent's self-satisfied monologue. The problem is that the natural reaction to this is to try and sound even more self-satisfied on your own turn by way of revenge.

This might sound like a group-dependent complaint. I don't believe it is.

RATING: 9
A total blast to play. Built on euro mechanics, but interactive to the point of being cutthroat. I will never turn down a chance to play this game. One of my absolute favorites.

It has solid resource management and dice placement mechanics, but also an extremely high level of interactivity. Not only the blocking typical of worker placement games, but stealing resources and cards, zapping your opponent's ships, cutting off their control of a region, and more.

You're rolling dice, and though there's no inbuilt chance mitigation system like the tools in Stone Age or the workers in Castles of Burgundy, you rarely feel screwed by the dice. Higher rolls can be used to block off the lunar mine, but low rolls can be used to get a better trading ratio at the orbital market. No matter what you roll, you can still use dice of any value to advance your colonies toward completion.

The retro-future theme really shines with excellent art, graphic design, and components. The factions expansion adds variable player powers, hidden agendas, and even more interactivity.

The game has a lot of depth and interactivity, but the basic premise is so simple (about the same complexity level as Stone Age or Lords of Waterdeep) that it's very easy to teach and learn.

Really I find very little to dislike about the game. It looks great, plays great, has enough depth and interaction to be replayed constantly but is simple enough to have wide appeal.

Edit: Upon subsequent plays, I've knocked this down from a ten. Kingmaking and leader screwage are the price of interactivity. I also can't think of a game of equivalent weight that has as much downtime. I'll be re-writing the above for a more balanced view.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Worker-placement game with a retro science fiction theme that uses dice as the "workers" (thematically, the dice are "ships"). There is also an area majority element provided by colony placement in territories. Special powers are obtained for controlling territories and owning "alien artifact" cards (powers include more efficient actions, dice manipulation and colony movements). 

At its core, the game centers on an engine for conversion of assets (energy, minerals, ships, colonies and alien artifact cards) into points, the goal being to end the game with the highest score - scoring is simple: one point for each placed colony plus one point for each controlled territory with the possibility of three additional bonus points obtainable from cards (two single victory point cards and cards that allow placement of a "positronic field" in a territory, giving a bonus point to the controlling player). Game ends immediately when a player plays his last colony - meaning that as scoring is open, the player ending the game is generally the winner (unless, unfortunately, a kingmaker is in the mix).

Game play flows smoothly and is fairly intuitive. There's a clever mechanism wherein placed dice are maintained in their spot until the player's next turn, causing action spaces to be continuously blocked. The game also has a significant level of interactivity as one must keep track of his relative score and can raid other player's resources and reposition colonies to improve his position. All of this result in a tense and tight game where the leader is often continuously changing. The bottom line is that Alien Frontiers is just plain fun. Best for 4 players in my opinion.

As an added bonus, components and retro sci-fi art are superb.

RATING: 8.5
Originally started playing this game on iOS. This might be one of the best iOS board games that I have with maybe only Stone Age or Carcassone being better. It's a pretty basic worker placement game that is way more difficult to learn than it is the play.

Having to explain the what each location allows you to do and the special powers of each location once you have the majority is a pain to new players. Games like this are a bit annoying because they really are super simple and quick to play once you understand everything (like 7 Wonders). However, I've played so much now that I can really give a good rating on how much I like this game. Although simple to play, there are many different paths to victory. Whether you go for cards or for more dice or for lessening resources,  you really can win any way. Can be a bit nasty as you can target certain players when stealing or placing communities, but this is mainly used to combat Kingbuilding. 

TL;DR - A much easier to play than teach worker placement game with a Sci-fi (fairly transparent) theme. One of the favorites in my collection.

RATING: 6
Excellent game.  The theme and gameplay mesh beautifully in such a way that you hardly ever think about the mechanics in terms of abstract game movement.  Although I am not a huge fan of dice, I love the use of dice in this game.  Instead of being good or bad, the options around the board give whatever you roll a viable strategy. *Edit - I have changed my rating of this game.  After many, many plays, I have decided that the game loses a bit of its luster after the first couple dozen plays.  Although I still believe that this is a very good game (and an excellent introduction into this style of game), the general same-ness and the downtime have gotten to me.  Perhaps it is a growing collection of very high quality games that has poisoned the well for me (Dominant Species, Dungeon Lords, 7 Wonders, Troyes, etc..), but this is no longer a game that I look forward to playing, when there are so many more dynamic and exciting options available.

RATING: 10
i own about a hundred board games, and Alien Frontiers is my favourite one of the bunch. i love the retro future art, the little domed colonies, and the unbridled ADVENTURE that the whole theme evokes. i love that the game switches halfway through from worker placement to territory control. i love all the fiddly manipulation the alien tech cards allow you to perform. 

i do NOT love how slow their second Kickstarter was, nor am i a fan of GameSalute and their obvious drive to produce a Big Box version while grinding their current fans under their wheels. It annoyed me tremendously that they announced a new Kickstarter campaign before they had even begun to fulfill the first one. i paid a bunch of extra money for ship-shaped dice that don't roll very well and weren't a good upgrade to the game. Love the game, dislike the company. The Kickstarter experience left a bad taste in my mouth, and i play Alien Frontiers almost begrudgingly now.

Az elso szuper nepszeru Kickstarter jatek volt, mondhatni ennek a farvizen indult neki a hobbijatekok kozossegi finanszirozasi modellje, ami mara mar szinte alapveto elofinanszirozasi mod.

A jatek maga egy erdekes worker placement, ahol d6 kockaink az urhajoink, amik dokkolnak bizonyos leteseitmenyekbe, hogy azokat hasznalva mi helyezhessuk el elsokent koloniainkat az uj bolygon. Viszont az egyes heylekre csak meghatarozott erteku kockak (pl. parok,tercek,sorok) tehetoek, szoval torekedni kell olyan strategia kialakitasara ami vagy hamar megszerzi a plusz kockakat vagy rugalmas a dobott ertekek vonatkozasaban.

Az idegen technologiak kartyai es az urkalozok direkt konfrontaciot engednek meg, raadasul elo a pontozas, azaz nem csak kapni, de vesziteni is lehet pontot - igy egy jatekos koreben akar tobbszor is atalakulhat az elmezony!

Minimalis hatrany, hogy csak 4 jatekos jatszhat, illetve, hogy a korodet nem lehet elore tervezni, mert az leginkabb a dobasodon mulik... ezert neha eleg sokat kell varni, amig a soron levo kitalalja, mit is akar kezdeni a korevel.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Commented after one game, which took 2 hours with almost newbie group. With four players that is too much, especially when there is quite a lot of downtime. You can plan your next turn, but if the dice do not co-operate or the previous player blocks the needed locations, you need to start planning again. This could be better with two or three. Not enough to do for such a long game anyway.

Dice game set in space, but the theme is quite thin. Lots of randomness (because the dice have a big role) and blocking others. It's mechanics are quite like Alea Iacta Est, if you are familiar with that game. A small reminder to Alhambra considering the downtime, because the previous player changes the board. :)

I'm not saying this game is bad, but it needs to played faster and may be with fewer players to be enjoyable.

RATING: 7.8
Due to the bash the leader element, the same strategy will never win this game twice in a row. For me, this makes the game BETTER, as you need to adapt your tactics to suit the current situation.

The people who say this game get repetitive are simply not creative enough to see their mistakes or not playing with people of skill.

The end game is always exciting as you must put yourself in a position for a home run move while at the same time manipulating the board to ensure you cannot lose if someone else ends the game.

RATING: 8
One 3p play and... its great! I love assigning dice, that's one of my favorite mechanics in a game, but I hate when the dice assigning doesn't feel like... dice. 

In this game, it lifts a few parts of yahtzee and blends in worker placement to make a very satisfying game. You want to roll high (Mostly) and you want duplicates or straights. Then it's just about how to assign those, with enough options at any one time that you don't feel like there's one obvious choice for every dice roll and you're torn in multiple directions. I felt like I had 2-3 avenues to go down in my play of this game, and each one seemed equally tempting and viable. That's a mark of a great, engaging game. 

I expect this to go to a 9 or maybe even 10 with more plays and/or trying the expansions.

RATING: 6
I guess I get the whole "Kingsburg in Space" comparisons, but really, this is a game that's separate enough from Kingsburg to be considered in its own light.  For one, the blocking element of Alien Frontiers gives it its own character; for another, the timing and flow of the game is very different.  Yes, it uses dice, and yes, you can place them at different places on the board based on what you roll, but the similarities end there.  Why dilute the effectiveness of a game by comparing it to something completely different?

1 play: 3 players
3 plays: 4 players

Do you have what it takes to be a deep space colonist? An alien frontier awaits the brave and daring! This new planet will be harsh, but if you have the skills to manage your resources, build a fleet, research alien life, and settle colonies, the world can be yours.
\13\\13\

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I think Kingsburg was the first game to bring dice into a worker placement game, but Alien Frontiers was the first to give this concept some teeth. Dice are your workers and every turn you roll them, to determine what their capabilities will be. Unlike Kingsburg, the choices here are not always obvious and more importantly, taking a space does not mean you have blocked it. Better rolls can knock you out, which adds a nice bit of push your luck to the formula. Obviously, there are means to mitigate and manipulate dice rolls, so as a strategy game Alien Frontiers works in much more interesting way than Kingsburg, right out of the box.

Dice filled Euros are all the rage right now, so this game seems a little less special than it used to be. Still, it remains a solid entry in the genre and there is a lot of fun to be had with it, even if you have played a dozen other dice placement games.

RATING: 8
Very good dice placement game that smoothly combines elements of area majority with special card abilities. The rules are very elegant and the iconography is clear and easy to digest. I was surprised by the amount of player interaction on the board - the game is full of "take that" moments so it definitely won't appeal to everyone.

The overall enjoyment is somewhat diminished by significant downtime between turns that gets longer and longer as the game progresses. There are times when you just get bored when your opponents try to figure out a perfect way of placing all of their dice (but I guess it all depends on the players - just try not to overthink your actions). 

Still, Alien Frontiers is a super solid, interesting design, check it out if you can. I would love to play it again, my rating will most probably go up with subsequent plays. I enjoyed this game a lot.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A solid first effort by a new game company.  Some people will love it, but it is just ok by my account.

In it's scoring, it is an area control game.  But, there isn't a lot happening on that front.  There really aren't enough pieces going on the board/moving around to really move control around very much.

So, what you really have is a dice based resource collecting/building game. My problem is that it takes a relatively long time to play what is fundamentally a pretty light game.  

Here's the problem.  There are about 10 places you can put your dice.  Some need high numbers, some low, some pairs, some trips, etc.  If you put dice in one place, you might get something that impacts your ability to use dice in another place.  Plus, everyone will ultimately have 2-4 cards that possibly let you manipulate the dice.  

When the game starts, you have 3 dice, so decisions are relatively easy.  However, later on, you have up to 6 (and possibly 7) dice to roll and place.  Which, of course, you really can't plan until you roll them.  So, when you roll all the dice, you need to stop and think about how to place them, add pips, get resources, etc. in a reasonable way.  Even for "fast" players this process can take a few minutes . . . while everyone else waits. 

Also, I think there is a bit of a rich get richer problem, where people who get additional dice early can really pull away quickly.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
This is probably my favorite game in the "worker placement" genre. Everything from the application of the theme to the excellent array of options for player interaction make this game fit my tastes just a bit more than other popular choices like Lords of Waterdeep or Kingsburg.

There are many different ways to strive for victory and, unlike some other games of its kind, if you find yourself constantly blocked by other players, there are a variety of ways to deal with it.

I like it over Kingsburg for the ability to grow your dice pool and for the variety of much more interactive cards and abilities and I like it over Waterdeep for the larger variety of ways to score your points.

An excellent game.

RATING: 8
Fun game.  Nice mechanics, where the dice roll dictate your possible actions.  Downside of this mechanic is that you cannot pre-plan your strategy with any certainty, so players must be able to adapt to Plan B or C.  Lots of interaction with blocking actions and stealing resources, cards, and territories from other players.  Because territories can switch possession, there usually isn't a runaway leader problem.  The game has a nice 2-hour length with 4 players, but AP-prone players can extend this by another hour.

RATING: 8.5
A very solid Worker-placement that oozes theme.  Great dice to placement mechanic with a lot of both strategic & tactical decisions.  You will be bumping up against other players very often - stealing their goods & taking their land, so you need to have a heart for that type of game.  

I personally really dig it, but it doesn't quite get the top tier of its category.  Analysis paralysis in this one is huge with people often wanting to "redo" their turns once they figure out the math in their head of what other possible scenarios are.  With this, the downtime between turns can be a bit frustrating.

Overall a solid B type of game.

RATING: 9
I'm very happy that I fell for Clever Mojo Games' "games remaining" countdown and placed a preorder. The publisher confirmed that I nabbed the 2nd to last copy. Phew! :)

Alien Frontiers is a blast to play. Sure there is some inherit downtime, as is common to dice games of this type (games where you roll all your dice at the start of your turn, then look over the board situation to see what you can do with them), but it's not too bad. We're a group prone to slow play, and we still easily finish this game in 60 minutes including rules explanation, and with repeat play ~45 is the norm.

When Alien Frontiers is played with players that embrace its competitive nature, its area control aspect, the worker placement nature of tactically placing your dice on the board, all seasoned with a good heaping of "take-that", then you have yourself a winner! This game delivers in a very satisfactory manner.

Even in defeat you always feel good about yourself, since you almost certainly pulled of some amazing combo during the game, that left you feeling particularly clever, and you can always blame your defeat on your poor luck, other players' good luck, or just them conspiring to keep you down. ;)

I consider Alien Frontiers a gamer's game, but it is still light enough that you can chat it up while playing. Normally this entails cheering for people to roll poorly, groaning as they block the space station you were depending on to use next turn, etc. This more than makes up for any downtime between turns.

In my opinion the game is not good with 2 players, it's great with 3 as long as you avoid double-teaming the 3rd player, and it's also quite good with 4 players. When playing 4-player games, I keep telling everyone to play as quickly as possible, otherwise the downtime can become a bother. I will never play the Long Version, where each player starts with 8 Colonies, again, it just drains the fun out of the game, and you can play two games in the time it takes to play the long version.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
[b]THE KING OF THE MOON:[/b]

1. Great space exploration theme.
2. Tight scoring. This leads into 3.
3. Well balanced.
4. Nice Dan Dare style art.
5. Subdued graphic design compliments the bright chunky dice extremely well.
6. Component quality is high.
7. Fun and interesting decisions. An enjoyable game.
8. I happen to like rolling and placing dice - this game does that by the atomic-shovel load.

[b]THE CHARCOAL BURNER ON THE DARK SIDE OF THE MOON:[/b]

1. Severe kingmaking. A few further games in and this is becoming even more acute - it's a real "ganking" experience.
2. Very tight scoring. Very tight.
3. Initially bewildering array of options on your turn, what with having to read the tech cards as well.
4. High screwage factor. Yes, you can bare grudges, crippling other players, possibly also losing the game to anyone involving themselves in grudge-wars.
5. No meaningful strategy. You simply cannot plan ahead as there are too many variables involved - other player's actions, and also the simple action of rolling dice each turn, adds considerable chaos.
6. Could be prone to AP - purely because you are unable to plan your turn in the downtime periods.
7. It seemed to me that as the game progresses, your tactical options narrow considerably. Funny when you consider that you will probably have more dice (spaceships) to place. This might be particularly acute when you have 5 bio-domes on the surface of the unnamed planetoid, and are unable, due to victory point standings, to place your last dome. It felt like there was little to do at this point, except go for alien technologies.
8. Straight away it is blindly obvious that the optimal positional plays on the planet is to only put 1 more bio-dome down than anyone else. There seemed no reward for concentrating on a region.
9. Dicefest and all that that entails. All the optimal play-thinking will do bollocks if you never roll any doubles - unlikely, but possible. Also, through no fault of your own, you could easily be blocked from doing any meaningful actions, rolls depending. Not fair to trash a dice game for having dice, however.
10. Space-Euro. When will somebody resurrect the AT-space genre?
11. Space Raiders might be the best option most turns, if you can roll a Yahtzee. This appears to be the case on repeated plays, as you elevate your position by using space pirates to steal from your opponents - whose position is lowered (it's a double gain). I tried this strategy out last game, using a tech card that altered a pip - which was good for creating straights 2-3-4, etc - and won fairly easily. Not sure if this is a good thing. It felt good.

RATING: 5.5
Kingsburgian, in that you roll your own personal set of dice and place them in different locations based on value and need.

The attack space is game-breakingly overpowered in my opinion.  Like the Settlers robber on steroids and pcp.  Might be a better game if it gets nerfed in house rules.

The rest of the spaces are creative and varied, with a couple valuable dumping grounds for bad rolls.

The area-control regions are all named after old scifi authors, a neat little easter egg.  The generic six-sided dice are rather ugly, and the game as a whole has a prototype look and feel.
